Election Notice*.
To the Federal Electors of Victoria.
Gentlemen, —
I beg to announce myself as a CANDIDATE
for one of the 10 seats assigned to Victoria in the
forthcoming CONVENTION, to be held to draft
a Constitution for the federation of the Australian
colonies. I shall be prepared to support a scheme
of federation on the following lines :
Under the Crown. —The union of the Aus-
tralian colonies indissolubly under the Crown.
Executive Government. The executive
government of the federation to be vested in a
Governor-General, appointed by the Queen, and
exercised by and through an administration re-
sponsible to the people represented in the Eederal
Parliament.
The Federal Parliament.—-To consist of two
Chambers—a Senate and a House of Representa-
tives—the Senate, or Upper House, being the
chamber in which the states will be equally repre-
sented ; the House of Representatives being the
popular chamber, or the National House, in which
the people of the federation will be represented by
members directly elected by them, each State re-
turning members in proportion to its population.
Limits of Federal Powers.— The Federal
Parliament and Government to be invested with a
power to deal -with foreign affairs, and certain
other specific and defined subjects of common
interest and general concern.
Autonomy of States. —The States to retain all
the powers, functions, and authorities now exer-
cised by them, except those which have been ex-
pressly transferred to the Federal Parliament and
Government.
Inter-Australian Free Trade.— Trade and
commercial intercourse between the States to be
absolutely free and unfettered upon the adoption
of a federal tariff by the Federal Parliament.
Foreign Trade. —The Federal Parliament to
have the exclusive power to impose duties of Cus-
toms upon the importation of goods from outside
countries, this being a power which, in my
opinion, would be exercised in the direction of
affording protection to Australian industries.
Railways. —The Federal Parliament to have
limited and defined control over railways for
federal purposes, especially to prevent the war be-
tween differential railway tariffs imposed by neigh-
boring provinces in order to attract trade from
one another.
Financial. —ln consideration of the transfer of
such revenue-earning departments as the Customs
and Excise and the Post and Telegraph, the federa-
tion to take over and be responsible for the public
debts and obligations of each State up to a stipu-
lated sum, the interest on which would be equal
to the revenue transferred.
Consolidation op Public Debts.—The federa-
tion to take steps to obtain a reduction in the rate
of interest on the consolidated public debts by a
scheme of conversion, under which it is estimated
that an annual saving in interest would be effected
amounting to .81,535,595. Of that sum the colony
of Victoria would gain ,8444,181. (See my article
on the “ Bathurst Federal Convention,” published
in the -Review op Reviews, 20th December, 1896.)
The additional federal expenditure, consequent on
the creation of a new Parliament and new execu-
tive and judicial departments, would not, it is
estimated, exceed the sum of .8230,000 per year.
Of that sum the Victorian proportion, according
to population, would be about £76,758. So that
the taxpayers would not have to bear any in-
creased burdens by federation, but, on the con-
trary, there would be large savings in the annual
interest bill more than sufficient to provide for
three times the above estimated increased expen-
diture.
Generally, I shall be prepared to promote
federation on democratic lines, coupled with
federal economy and provincial retrenchment.
Plan of Campaign.— Owing to the largeness of
the constituency to which I now appeal, as well as
the limited time and means at my disposal, I shall
be unable to engage in a personal canvas, but I
shall endeavor to address public meetings in
several of the large centres, when I hope to have
the opportunity of more fully expounding my
views.
I am, Gentlemen,
Yours obediently,
JOHN QUICK, LL.D.
Bendigo, 19th January, 1897.

Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting held at the Banking House, George-street, Sydney,

Presented to the Shareholders at the General Meeting of the Company held in the Exchange, Sydney, on
FRIDAY, 22nd JANUARY, 1897.
The Directors have pleasure in submitting their Nineteenth Annual Report, with the audited accounts for the
year ending 31st December, 1896 :

The Half-yearly Dividend to Shareholders in July absorbed £750, and after providing £1563 6s. 3d. for payment
of Bonuses to Policyholders on Premiums paid during the year there remains at credit of Profit and Loss Account a

To Reserve Fund, making that fund now £13,500 ... ... ... ... ... £l5OO 0 0

The Directors are pleased to report continued progress in the business of the Company, and a substantial increase
in the Reserve Fund, which now stands at £13,600.
The Total Funds now amount to £29,373, being equal to 228 per cent, of the Premium Income, and are safely
invested in first-class interest-bearing securities. The amount which would be required to reinsure current risks
is £4300.
‘ A Branch has been established in Melbourne for the transaction of business in the Colony of Victoria under the
management of Mr. Robert A. Paxton. The expense incurred in opening the branch has been written off in the
Profit and Loss Account.
The Directors desire to express their sincere regret at the death of their esteemed colleague, Mr. James P. Garvan,
founder of the Company, and to record their acknowledgement of the valuable services rendered by him to the Com-
pany since its formation.
The Shareholders have now to elect Two Directors in the room of Patrick Stanley, Esq., J.P., and John Donelan,
Esq., who retire by rotation, but are eligible for re-election, and have given notice of their candidature.
It will also be necessary to elect Two Auditors in the place of Thomas Miller, Esq., and W. G. Winton, Esq., only
one of whom is eligible for re-election.
Messrs. W. G. Winton, J. Roberts, and W. Dolman have given notice that they are candidates foe the position of
Auditor.
The Dividend to Shareholders will be payable at the Offices of the Company on the termination of the General
Meeting. The Bonus to Insurers is payable on Renewal of Policies.
JOHN SEE, Chairman.

SPECIAL DISEASES.
He has, by diligent research, investigated their

profession. The various diseases embraced in his
speciality are those which have hitherto caused
more misery and suffering than any other kind of
trouble that affects the human frame.
Young Men
who suffer from of the following symptoms,
namely : —Frequent headaches, giddiness, drowsi-

feeling of fullness after eating, specks before the
eyes, halos or circles round a candle, cold hands
and feet, pains in the back or under the shoulder-
blade, tenderness of the breast-bone on pressure,
palpitation of the heart, clammy hands, an
aversion to society, a fear of something dreadful
about to happen, impure blood, spots or
pimples on the back, chest, legs, or face, failing
memory, hacking cough, falling of the hair, &c.,
&c., should consult him, for he is now enabled
quickly and effectually, to remove the cause, and

All Sorts Suited
Perhaps you are one who only thinks of the
UTILITIES, one who eschews everything but
what is strictly necessary. You’ve a penchant
for FLANNELS, but have a DERRY on SILKS,
and when there’s HOMESPUN about you can’t
see the occasion for bothering about PLUSH and
VELVET. Simple and good is your motto ;
you’re almost a Quaker, though not quite, and
you’d rather see your money in the bank than on
your back, and dividends are better, say you, than
Ribbons and Laces. Well, have your own way.
We provide for all sorts and sizes.
Or perhaps you are of another class. A world-
ling, if you will, floating down the stream of life,
with “ youth at the prow and pleasure at the
helm,” you want to dress well and to look well.
The eyes of the fair are upon you, and you would
win their smiles. You want Clothes and Hats
and Boots, Gloves you must have, and a thousand
other things you crave for. Rings, Lockets,
Gems, exquisite Neckties, Shirts galore, good
CIGARS, and TOBACCO of the best. You’d
like them all, wouldn’t you ? You can have them,
sir, at our store for what is, when compared to
what other stores are asking, a mere song.

Various Verses .
Item of evidence in a Maoriland divorce-
case: “We’ve been married 22 years; she is no
kind of a housekeeper at all. When I’ve fancied
a glass of beer I couldn’t get it. She has seen my
tongue hanging out for want of one. Yet I love
her still.”

Tynan, the much-advertised “No. 1” of
the Fenian Brotherhood, whose recent burlesque
arrest was cabled all over the world, is thus de-
scribed by New York World
Tynan, nearly blind, a feeble, broken-down man, never
much more than a prematurely-aged baby, has been for
fourteen years the bugaboo by which English mothers
have scared their crying children to sleep. Of all the
men who might be chosen by conspirators froni this
country to commit the crime of bomb-throwing, Tynan
would be absolutely the last. In the first place, his claim
to notoriety rests entirely on his own loudly-proclaimed
assertions that he is the “No. 1” who.figured in the
Phoenix Park murders. Nine-tenths of the Irishmen in
America know that his claim to that doubtful distinction
is absurd, and that he was never more than a messenger
between John Walsh— “ Stockport, the real No 1
and the “ Invincibles,” who slew Lord Cavendish and
Under-Secretary Burke. When Tynan arrived in this
country in 1884 he found that the personality of No. 1
was still a mystery, so far as the general public was con-
cerned, and he being a man of somewhat quick resource,
immediately assumed the role, and Americans and Irish-
men who were not on the ground-floor accepted him.
Tynan got along for a time. He never prospered in any
sense because his spare moments were largely devoted to
posing He was a tremendous poser. He occupied the
centre of the stage, and the limelight played on him
whenever four or five Irishmen met to talk at a bar.
WHEN THE SEPARATOR SINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the early summer morning,
When the air is sweet and still,
Oh, I watch for Elsie coming,
After milking, down the hill,
Lifting lilting tunes so merry,
And love lightens up the land,
When she’s singing through the tussocks
With a pail in either hand.
When she goes into the dairy,
Love goes too, on lissom wings,
For he knows of kisses coming
When the separator sings.
When the separator sings
With its burr-urr-urr,
When the separator sings
With its whirr-irr-irr —
If love’s laughter you’d be learning,
And would feel love’s kisses burning,
Then just try the handle-turning
When the separator sings !
In the early winter dawning,
When the dew is on the grass,
Oh, I watch, as Elsie’s coming,
Grass-blades lean to let her pass ;
And her gold hair gilds the morning,
And her skirts uplifted be,
Just a bit above her ankle
And a bit below her knee.
As we cuddle in the dairy,
Greater we than queens or kings—
Love-land’s emperor and empress,
When the separator sings.
When the separator sings
With its burr-urr-urr,
When the separator sings
With its whirr-irr-irr,
Oh, the lore of love we’re learning,
And love’s guerdon we are earning,
While the handle’s turning, turning
When the separator sings 1
Winter, summer, every morning,
Neither drought nor dew can daunt;
From the dairy every dawning
Elsie’s heart and mine will jaunt
To the years of joyous wedlock
In the happy days to be,
When I shall have married Elsie,
And she will have married me.
In the future by our fireside,
When we’ve heard our bridal clang,
Won’t we bless the merry mornings
When the separator sang ?
When the separator sang
With its burr-urr-urr,
When the separator sang
With its whirr-irr-irr,
When love’s laughter we were learning,
And we felt love’s kisses burning,
As the handle I was turning
When the separator sang l
STEELE GREY.
A MOTHER.
A withered wee old woman comes in dreams
From forth the mirage-desert-land of Death,
And in her haggard eyes for ever beams
The that is fanned by God s own
breath ;
And when the track ahead is tramped by men
Who scorn Dishonor as a loathsome load
One hand clasps mine. A hopeful kiss-—and then
The other points me up the mountain-road !
And when at times, where mountain pathways
meet,
Adown a shady bridal-track I gaze
To where some fair Sin with suggestion sweet
Half-hides and beckons through the forest haze,
She comes between, her hands tight-clasped in
fear,
Her old face puckered into pained seams,
Her eyelids throbbing with the unshed tear,
Sweet, withered, wee old woman of my dreams !
Vic.
L.M.D.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 6, 1897.
Signs of Prosperity.
The year which has just commenced promises
to be one of magnificent borrowing. Six out
of the seven provinces have loans in view,
most of them large ones, and the signs are
that 1897 is to be the wildest loan-boom year
that Australasia has seen since the early ’Bo’s.
Westralia proposes to raise £7,000,000, and
have such an orgy on the proceeds as no
other community of its size ever had since
the fall of Babylon, or some such remote
date. New South Wales is a good second
with a scheme for raising nearly £5,000,000
in all. Queensland is planning a loan of
over 2 : j- millions ; Maoriland is borrowing
£1,000,000 ; Victoria and Tasmania £250,000
each ; and only South Australia stands out—
though it may also rush to the front later on.
The total, so far, is £15,700,000, which is a
rather considerable outbreak for a population
of a little over 4,000,000 souls, and is sug-
gestive of the worst days of the loan-drunk
which raged during the last decade. It was
a great and glorious drunk while it lasted,
when New South Wales borrowed and
“ blewed ” millions inside three years,
and Queensland got through nine millions in
four years, and Victoria dissipated 11| mil-
lions in the same period. Those good days
came to a sudden stoppage about ’9l, when
the British lender had a cold fit of funk,
hence depression settled down on Australia,
and it sat in sackcloth and scraped itself
sadly, like Job, with a potsherd. There was
much talk of economy about that time, and
of swearing-off and being a good, self-respect-
ing community ; but now that the British
capitalist has again money to lend, the riot
recommences as gaily as ever, and six hogs
in a row have returned to their wallow.
* * * *
Australian booms, since the first great
gold deposits gave out, have all been built
more or less on borrowed money. The great
rivalry between New South Wales and Vic-
toria is a case in point. Going by the census
periods, the latter province was far ahead in
every respect up till 1881, and kept pretty
well abreast till 1891. With only one-
quarter the area it had, till 1881, 100,000
more people, as large a revenue, more immi-
grants, as much commerce and shipping,
more railways, agriculture, manufactures, a
bigger capital city, and more boom of every
kind. Also it had a debt of 22 millions
against the older province’s 17 millions.
Then, from 1881 to 1896, the mother pro-
vince, as its orators are so fond of pointing
out, overtook its competitor with ‘ ‘ giant
strides.” It got ahead in population, in
shipping, in revenue, and it got vastly ahead
in immigration. In these 15 years ending
in 1896, New South Wales borrowed
£45,000,000 against Victoria’s £25,000,000,
and the province with the biggest inrush of
loan-money had the biggest boom as usual.
From 1892 to 1895 Victoria only added
about £200,000 to its debt. This was the
first time it had practically sworn off in 20
years, and, as the natural result, the loan-
dipsomaniac province went through an awful
process of recovery, which is only now show-
ing signs of being over. If Victoria keeps
off its ancient dissipation the recovery will
not have to be repeated ; if it gives way
again to the old drunkenness its next attempt
to swear off will be marked by the same pain-
ful monetary swelled head in the morning,
and the agony will have to be gone through
all over again. In the four years New South
Wales borrowed £7,000,000, therefore,
though it was depressed, having been accus-
tomed to borrow twice or three times that
amount, it wasn’t nearly so depressed as the
province which had sworn off altogether.
Under these conditions, it viewed its neigh-
bor with scorn, and claimed, and still claims,
much superior merit. New South Wales
has its recovery still to do, and, having been
on the borrow every year for 23 years, it
doesn’t yet know what the painful process is
like.
* * * *
The only years —there were about four of
them, all told—when the New South Wales
railways paid their way were years of tremen-
dous borrowing ; when the borrowing dimin-
ished the boom faded, and the railways
promptly became a losing investment, and
have remained that way ever since. The
Victorian railways had a brief spell of profit
in the early ’Bo’s, when the province made
its last big effort at the part of the prodigal
son and borrowed £9,000,000 in four consecu-
tive years ; but they have never got there, or
even anywhere near there, since. South
Australia made a substantial profit on its
lines for several years—the ones when it was
borrowing over a million per annum ; now it
is borrowing on a very small scale, and its
lines are in the general category of deficits.
The only paying railway system in the seven
provinces is now that of Westralia, which is
also the most frantic borrower of the seven.
Likewise, Westralia is the only province
with a boom. This year it is anticipated
that Westralia will raise a little over one
million of gold out of its mines and about
six millions out of John Bull, and
the result will be described, in the usual
phrase, as the Westralian gold-boom, and
hardly anybody will notice that only one-
seventh is gold and six-sevenths are paper
and I O U and burdens handed down to
posterity. Westralia, by the way, is the
only province whose circumstances make it
possible to raise a decent borrowing boom of
the old dazzling kind, and when it bursts
there will be nothing big remaining, until
New Guinea and the Northern Territory
have grown large enough to go on the tear
upon their separate account. Away back in
’B4 New South Wales was accustomed to
raise 5| millions in a year, and only a little
over one-fifth of it went out in interest,
leaving more than four millions to create a
great burst of prosperity in the country.
Now the borrowings are about two millions
a year, and it all goes out in interest on the
enlarged debt, and nothing remains to create
what is commonly called prosperity. There-
fore there is very little prosperity, and there
is going to be less. Only a general borrow-
ing of 6f millions per annum from now till
the end of the century could make New
South Wales what it was a decade ago.
Westralia, not having accumulated a very big
interest bill as yet, is just now where its
neighbors were about 16 years ago. A few
years lienee it will probably be where they
are now, which is a very different situation.
* * * *
The great I O U business is deteriorating
in character as well as in size. The primary
object of borrowing for many years back has
been to furnish employment. The first
great outbreak of railway construction
created a boom in the labor-market, and
brought a large increase of population.
When the works were finished there was no
employment for the discharged laborers, so
more works were started to keep them em-
ployed. When all the railways which pro-
mised to be immediately profitable were
finished, unprofitable ones were started.
So long as the unemployed could be
absorbed and poverty jkept down and depres-
sion put out of sight by railway building
there was some semblance of sanity about
the proceeding. It was not openly and
avowedly a case of relief works, but of
“opening up the country ” and “developing
the national resources,” and things like that”
Now the railway-building cannot be con-
ducted on the old scale of magnificence
because it is already ahead of population in
many places; if the unemployed question
was solved and the depression lifted in the
old heroic way it would necessitate construct-
ing hundreds of miles of line that wouldn’t
pay working expenses. The interest on bor-
rowed money would be one loss, and the
deficit on working another, so the cash would
be thrown away twice over. Therefore,
Premier Reid now borrows for statues and
repairs, and for sand-shifting and forest-
thinning and road-making and scrub-cutting
purposes ; and Premier Seddon borrows for
roads to open up the scenery of Maori-
land so that the debilitated tourist can
view the beauty spots of the province;
and Premier Turner borrows for the pur-
pose of cutting tracks in Gippsland. The
business is getting down to be, in the
main, pauper relief with no disguise what-
ever. There is no legal enactment in any of
the provinces setting forth what may justly
be charged to loans, and what should be
debited to revenue, so this matter depends
wholly on the unscrupulousness or incom-
petence or mendacity of the Government and
its majority. There is no limit to the
amount which may be borrowed in any one
year. There is no law requiring that the
additional debt incurred shall bear any rela-
tion to the increase of population, so as to
prevent the burden on each inhabitant grow-
ing larger; and even in these days of com-
parative moderation the liability on each
Australian grows larger every year. It was
£34 per head in 1881, and in 1895, despite
15 years of the most rapid population-in-
crease in the country’s history, it reached
£52 per head. Altogether there are many
possibilities in front of this great country.
Three years more at the present gorgeous
rate should see the last survivor of the
booms—that of Westralia—about through,
Then Australasia should be in a position to
sit down among the ruins, and look back on
its first complete century—the fastest first
century in any new country’s record—and
think deeply about all the joys and sorrows,
the booms and bursts and glories and panics
—that it has compressed into that brief
period, and wonder thereat.
That Federal Capital.
“ Fluffy ” to The Bulletin :
As a Victorian of the most bigoted and persecuting
type, I want to mention that there are only two proposals
for a Federal capital that are worth mentioning. The
Bulletin’s special fancy for a location half-way between
the Wallaby Track and the Never Never Land I look down
upon with the generous pity of a right-thinking person.
As I said, there are only two schemes —Sydney Jephcott’s
and Mine. But The Bulletin’s idea is not bad as a flight
of fancy—supposing we eliminate all the practical diffi-
culties and unpleasantnesses. So far back as 25th Aug.,
’94, J. G. Drake (Q.) unbosomed himself of a Marble City
mid-way between Bourke and Charleville, built on a
block of 50,000 sq. miles taken around the spot where
three provinces (Q., N.S.W., and S.A.) meet. J. G. D.
assures us that the climate is all that can be desired,
Bourke having a mean summer temperature J^deg. less
than Cairo and 4deg. less than New Orleans. (How much
copier than Hay, Hell, and Booligal is not stated.) The
idea is that railways will be built to this cool corner, so
that perspiring Australians might take a journey thither
as a pick-me-up. The most surprising thing about
this scheme is that it was afterwards adopted
by The Bulletin. The “ further edge of nowhere,
on the road to the setting sun,” the “sultry
regions of Western Queensland among the head waters of
the Gregory River,” were gravely set forth as offering a
chance for a Federal capital that would appease jealousies,
give the railways an object in life, offer an opportunity
for an experiment in land nationalisation, scatter the
population a little more, and place the machinery of
Government, Treasury, and the State arsenals out of
reach of the inevitable invader. Pom, pom ! It is the
present writer's belief that this invader person will dodge
Australia for fear of getting mixed up in the Fiscal Issue
—but supposing that he will come, he won’t want Trea-
sury ledgers, State documents, and the visitors’-book
from Government House. He will want Cash, and will
find it in the bank-vaults of Sydney and Melbourne.
Moreover, if Central Mount Stuart is to be made the State
arsenal, then the State forts may as well be shifted there
also. Lately The Bulletin has been mentioning Port
Augusta as the ideal spot for the capital. The writer does
not pretend to know where Port Augusta is, but it is so
obviously a resort of sand-flies, mixed Asiatics, and
suicide as to be out of the question.
* • * »
The Sydney Conference postponed the Capital question
—“ left it open ”—as something too definite and danger-
ous for the generalities of Federal agreement. There has
been one self-denying ordinance. Bathurst has offered to
withdraw its claims. But there are plenty of claimants.
Albury has agreed enthusiastically that the capital should
be near Wodonga, on the N.S.W. side of the river
'w 0 ? 1 (Yia)> Launceston (Tas.), and Newcastle
(N.S.W.) feel .sure, that if merit had any chance there is
only one place in it. Ballarat has decided that its central
position on the railway from Geelong (near Vic.) to
Adelaide is a distinct from Heaven. Adelaide waits
in holy meekness, confident of the result. Geelong
agrees that the question would be settled by dredging
Conoßay. Meanwhile, Sydney and Melbourne frankly
watch one another, waiting for the coming struggle that
will settle political, if not commercial, supremacy.
• * * *
_ SVIJN’EY Jefhcott’s scheme is to have no Federal capital
a , fc ,■ seat of Federal Government in Australasia
should not be fixed, but movable.” Jephcott proposes
that each provincial capital in the Federation should be
recognised in turn. Legislators would assemble one
year in Sydney, next in Melbourne, next in Adelaide.
[Presumably the Government offices would be dragged
round on wheels per bullock.-ED. B.] If the MaSri-
lander and the Westralian were included in such a
scheme, neither could be chilled by the idea that his pro-
? , bec . o ™® a f c 'P he . r the sum of Australasian
nationality. At first hearing the plan sounds bizarre.
Australians are so chained to old-world conventions that
they wear bell-toppers in their broiling summer, build
houses on the ideas evolved in the congested cities of
semi-Arctic countries, and import most of their literary
and all of their dramatic opinions from London. England
has a fixed capital. Therefore, Australia must have a
fixed capital. England eats hot plum-pudding at Christ-
mas therefore Australia, &c. In the Jephcott proposal
the Federal legislator would not be subject to one city’s
influence from year to year. If he held office for as many
years as there were capitals in the Federation, he would
become as familiar with the life and spirit of the other
capitals as with his own. I have talked this scheme over
. ?w’ u'! d he , ? ees nothing much against it except
this . that Jim would never agree to it. This, I am afraid
is fatal. Bill is not content to vote for a thing he wants
himself-he must be convinced that Jim wants it also
Likewise, Jim refuses to support any notion that doesn’t
strike him as being acceptable to Bill.
• * * * '
What are the essentials of a Federal capital, supposing
we must have one ? For one thing, it must be a
place to live in, so that legislators shall not pose before
the country as martyrs in the cause. If we hive it in f
thinly-populated locality, there is a danger that the politi-
cal ideas of members may become subordinated to Social
necessities. What Murray Smith calls the “club feeling
which always exists in such bodies ” as legislatures,
dangerously by banishing the leg slators
‘° a Picturesque isolation , n some geometrically-corre”
centie of Australasia. The danger would manifest itself
in emasculating debates of those severe and searching-
criticisms that make our rulers tread warily, would result
in toleration of abuses rather than the ruptnre nf tv!!
friendships that made the place of exile tolerable In a
nw‘ ty ’ 'u e ' nbers fin . d con Kenial circles outside" Parfia
ment each man remains more or less himself, and does
not become merely an approximation to a comfortable
average !n a big city there is mental activity—a nervous
sj stem that responds sympathetically to big ideas and
big movements in the cause of humanity a"J
these is the press of a big city—Sydney or Melbourne, e.g.
—which is alert, critical, many-voiced. The communica-
tor from Parliament to people—and from people to Parlia-
ment. Criticising, castigating, belauding parliamentary
doings for the benefit of the public.. Reflecting, creat-
ing, transmuting, dissecting public opinion for the benefit
of Parliament. The mathematical mean of the forces
represented by Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Wellington,
Adelaide Hobart, and Perth is as absurd as a logical con-
clusion if it be fixed 1000 miles away from every great
newspaper. If we must have a fixed capital, let it not be
a little village that, in session, would be a habitation for
bored legislators and bureaucrats, and, in recess, an
empty tomb haunted by a scared caretaker or two, in-
structed to frighten away the dingoes.
* * • •
Considered as big cities, most of the provincial capitals
are failures. Only two, Melbourne and Sydney, fill the
bill. [Adelaide may now consider itself abolished.]
After all, what a city Melbourne is! It has a smelly
river, and a sanitary system that makes the night a thing
that can be felt—like the darkness of Egypt. These are
on one side of the ledger ; on the other side must be put
the fact that it is a city of enjoyments. Its tramway and
railway systems are almost perfect, as far as the comfort
of the passengers is concerned. It has the finest race-
course in the world. Its best theatre is the best in Aus-
tralia. It is the musical centre of Australia. Its news-
papers are close to top-notch. It is a city of ideas, and
of men who think, and there is an intellectual contagion
in its air (as well as other contagions, did you say ?). If
you would judge the influence of Melbourne, look for it
in Sydney trade and commerce, and see what Melbourne
men have done and are doing there.
* • * *
Melbourne is indisputably the best place in Australia
for the capital—bar Sydney. In November Melbourne
has a great attraction for visitors, and its streets are
thronged with folk from all corners of the Australias—
and even the alleged Australian Navy finds time to give it
a cali. But Melbourne is not a city for sightseers. We
want a Federal capital that will be attractive to the legis-
lators —but it is also extremely desirable that a tide of
the electors should flow through it, to intensify the pub-
licity that is so essential to the good conduct of public
business. We want the Federal capital to be complete at
all points—and beauty of situation is a principal one.
That Yankee tornado, Talmage, described Sydney Harbor
as the magnificent gateway of Australia. The Harbor is
such a compendium of exquisite beauties that it touches
every beholder —the more shallow of whom attempt
to disguise their feeling by uttering bad jokes. I
don’t think that even The Bulletin has made
a good joke about the Harbor. That was funny
about the syndicate wanting to tunnel beneath
it to North Shore, so that people could go by ’bus to busi-
ness, instead of by ferry. But that wasn’t a joke on the
Harbour, it was a joke on the Sydney newspapers. As
regards situation, Sydney is incomparable. The approach
to Melbourne from the sea is the dreariest, dirtiest per-
formance. Port Phillip Bay has all the disadvantages of
the ocean, with none of its beauties. Hobson’s. Bay is a
partial relief—then comes the obscure river-passage, with
its ludicrous sign-boards, “ Dead-Slow,” to suggest the
facts of the case to the unobservant traveller. Or, you
land at Port Melbourne and go by rail along the back of
the back-yards of an ugly suburb. From Sydney Heads
to Sydney the steamer is an item in a transformation-
scene —one beauty melting into and outrivalled by the
next. Nature made up her mind about the Federal
Capital before even the kangaroo was evoluted. The site
has been prepared with such care that, apparently, man
cannot spoil it. Even Pinchgut, horrible anywhere else,
becomes picturesque in Sydney Harbour. And yet we
hear of men going about talking as if any other place
could be the capital!
PLAIN ENGLISH.
British Capital.
A week or two ago the Bendigo (Vic.) Amal-
gamated Miners’ Association carried a resolu-
tion that the Government should despatch an
official boomster to England “ with the object
of inducing British capitalists to invest in
Victorian mines.” The Age has also lifted its
voice on several occasions to the effect that
outside capital should be attracted to Vic-
torian mines, and the usually level-headed
Turner Government now cables sensational
mining returns to London—also with a view of
attracting British capital. The Bulletin
moves as an amendment upon this that Vic.
should pass a law prohibiting any Victorian
mine being owned by any person or syndicate
or co. outside of Australasia, and that British
capital arriving for investment should be
treated on the same principles as phylloxera or
tick. This country's state securities are now
mostly owned in England, likewise a lot of its
municipal securities, its banks, insurance com-
panies, its city lands, pastoral and agricul-
tural ditto, its bricks and mortar, mortgages,
shipping interests, shares in all manner of
companies—-in fact, the whole country is more
or less owned by or mortgaged to England.
The proposal that a deliberate effort should be
made to get the British claw on the only im-
portant industry that is yet mostly Australian
is a wondrous slab of foolishness. Victoria
can, no doubt, attract British capital if it sells
its mines at prices which will enable the J.
Bull man to make a profit, but a much simpler
and more beautiful idea is to keep them and
make the profit itself. The said profit is wanted
badly enough. A big inrush of British capital
might get the gold out faster, but when the
question lies between getting it out faster for
the benefit of J. Bull or getting it out more
slowly for the benefit of the Australian himself,
the latter idea seems to have something to
recommend it. Whether the country sells its
mines, or its pastoral or agricultural lands, or
its city properties, to J. Bull and has a tem-
porary boom with the money, or whether a
private citizen sells his house and has a great
outbreak of champagne and jewellery and
lottie with the proceeds till it is
gone, the result is much the same. Either
way it is the spendthrift policy of sell-
ing the regular income of the future
tor a day or two of garish boom and howl in the
present; it is the story of the prodigal son—
the Israelite that failed—over again without
variations. For the country which has no gold
worth speaking of the idea of attracting British
capital to its mining industry has much to re-
commend it regarded as plain swindle on a large
scale; for the country which has gold it is
ruinous. British capital attracted to the land-
jobbing and banking business landed Victoria
in tbe great boom and the heartrending smash
of a few years ago. British capital-if there is
enough of it—attracted to the mining industry
may easily do the same a few years hence ; and
the spectacle of the Victorian who is just re-
covering from one historic burst trying already
to lay the foundations of another is a dis-
mal evidence of the shortness of the public
memory. Also, the spectacle of a country,
which believes in Protection as a means of
developing local industries and making the
community self-reliant, craving after im-
ports of foreign capital and wanting to rely
on the foreign capitalist, is somewhat sorrowful.
It Victoria developes its own gold-industry as
it has done in the past, the profits of it, dis-
tubuleU locally, will see it through many


seasons of depression for years and years to
come. If it booms furiously on British capital
a number of people will make big fortunes
trading off properties to J. Bull, and many of
them will go to Europe with their pile to make
acquaintance with dukes and other animals ;
and thereafter the mining-dividends will go to
England to pay interest on the other money that
has gone to England also, and Victoria will lose
the interest without getting much of the prin-
cipal. Then the British manager will come out
and eject the local man from his billet, and
wages w ill be cut down to make dividends on
the huge nominal capital with which the British
Stock Exchange robber always burdens his
mining-enterprises, and in one way and another
the Bendigo Amalgamated Miners’ Association
will have cause to wish it had never been born.
The Case of Cecile Andersen.
There is a fatality which haunts the people
who decide to “die together”; in probably
three instances out of four the episode pans out
the same way—one is taken and the other left.
The latter is generally left for the hangman or
the gaol, the law mostly assuming that the
person who survived was the one mainly re-
sponsible. The Middle Park (Melb.) tragedy,
where the episode was left unfinished in the
usual fashion, has served to draw attention to
another Melb. case of a few years back —that
of Mrs. Andersen —which was more or less of
the same order. In regard to this matter a
Melb. correspondent writes :
Presuming that your readers have a general idea of the
circumstances which led up to the tragedy, I will state
the facts as they occurred on the 19th June, 1894. Fraser
and Mrs. Andersen sat on a large-sized sofa in a room by
themselves, Fraser at the right-hand end and Mrs.
Andersen at the other end. At 10 o’clock two shots,
fired in succession, were heard, and three persons who
had been only a few feet away hurst into the room. They
found Fraser and Mrs. Andersen still seated as described,
both apparently dead— Fraser with a bullet wound in
his left temple, and Mrs. Andersen shot behind her right
ear. The cape of Mrs. Andersen’s dress was thrown over
her head, and a revolver was lying on the floor in front of
Fraser, several feet from Mrs. Andersen. Fraser was
quite dead, but Mrs. Andersen, on being removed to the
hospital, recovered. It was proved that the bullet had
been fired at Fraser’s temple “ from above and behind.”
The medical evidence alleged that Mrs. Andersen’s wound
caused instantaneous insensibility ; nevertheless, to make
her guilty she must not only have fired that shot at
Fraser, but, after having next shot herself, she must
have placed the revolver several feet away, and then
thrown her cape above her head and awaited the denoue-
ment. All this looks like a physical impossibility. I am
indebted to the late Lormer Board here for a very apt
Eh rase, which enables me to criticise the trial without
eing guilty of contempt of court, viz., “unconscious
bias.”
The case turned entirely on the question
whether Mrs. Andersen shot Fraser or
whether Fraser shot her, or whether they
attempted two separate and individual suicides
by mutual agreement, and the evidence looks a
little worse in the first-named direction than in
either of the others. Anyhow, there was a very
large-sized doubt, and the prisoner did not get
the benefit of it. She was sent to gaol and
narrowly escaped being sent to the gallows on
a miserably weak case. Probably the thing
above all others which wrecked her chances was
the fact that she was a Woman with a Past,
and her Past, expounded at great length in the
daily papers, aroused the soul of the great
aggregate Podsnap against her. Fraser also
had a Past —the same one as Mrs. Andersen’s ;
they had apparently shared it together —-but
the kind of respectability which is symbolised
in a shiny top hat takes no notice of such things
except in a woman. Mrs. Andersen’s case is
only one of many which go to show the neces-
sitj' of a court of criminal appeal in Australia.
The system by which the litigant in a small
matter of property can carry his case from one
court to another and appeal again and again,
while the one whose life is at stake has only
one toss-up with the hangman, is an infamy.
The N.S.W. Timber “Trade.”
A WELL-INFORMED correspondent: —
Now is the time when you will read in the N.S.W.
coastal press glowing accounts of the revival of the timber-
trade in half-a-dozen districts. Details of the “ pros-
perity’’thus produced are never given. Let me record
them, taking a typical case. Victoria (say) calls for
tenders for some big national work. Specifications state
that the timber must be well-seasoned Australian hard-
wood or ironbark girders, so many inches in diameter,
square, or whatever the case may be. Blank & Co. are
Victorian or S.A. contractors, and submit a tender, which
in due course is accepted. They have made all necessary
inquiry, and know exactly where to go for the timber.
Of course, they come into N.S.W., where an asinine Govt,
will give them timber for the taking of it away. They
erect their mill on one of the rivers, on a site obtained
from Govt, for a few pounds a year rent. In a very short
time the bush around resounds with the echoes of the axe.
The local paper prints a column congratulating the district
on its good fortune in having a new industry started in its
midst, and points out that the shipping-trade will be con-
siderably increased. Soon Blank and Co. have a load
of timber ready for their schooner, which is daily ex-
pected from Victoria in ballast. What does the ballast
consist of? Flour, oats, potatoes, chaff, &c., from Victoria
or S.A. for the use of Blank and Co.’s men. Meanwhile,
the best of the waste and flitch pieces have been utilised.
Blank and Co. have had a couple of dead-beats erecting
buildings which are sold or rented to employes at the
mill. Blank and Co. build a store to hold the schooner’s
stock, and retail it at prices from 10 to 25 per cent, above
local values. The men soon learn the consequence of not
buying at the mill-store. In a very short time the bulk
of the employes are in debt to the mill-owners, and con-
tinue so till the end of the chapter. The majority of
mill hands get rid of the few shillings which may
fall into their hands very easily by dint of hard
drinking and gambling. Consequently, any out-
sider who may be unfortunate enough to have
money owing him from them has Buckley’s show of
getting it. If he sues the debtor, the debtor has nothing,
and if he garnishees the employers he finds that they are
also creditors. All this time the timber-cutting is going
on briskly. For a month or two the cutters hold licenses
and are able to produce them when the Govt, forester
happens along, but presently this way of working is found
inconvenient, and the mill-owners undertake to obtain
the necessary licenses and keep them at the office for the
men. The whole of the district, which may comprise
several millions of acres, is under the control and super-
vision of one forester, and, of course, he cannot be every-
where at once, and may possibly visit the locality where
Blank and Co. are working once in three or four months.
When he does, and finds the men working in the bush, he
is referred to the mill-owners for the production of the
licenses. The chances are ten to one that the mill-owners
have omitted to take licenses out for that month, though
they have not omitted to debit the cutters’ accounts with
the necessary fee. Of course the affair is fixed up between
Blank and Co. and the forester - the firm being cor
sidered above suspicion. The cutting is still going on,
and I will guarantee there is not a suitable tree within
the working radius which is left standing. Reserves
or private property —it is all the same. To-day the trees
are standing : to-morrow they are gone, and no one
knows who felled them. An application to. the mill-owners
brings no satisfaction. They do not know who cut the
trees or who drew them in. If by any chance they are
caught in the act, ignorance is the plea ; and if the poor
cockv who owns the land is anyway easy they soon ob-
tain his consent to let the log go to the mill by promising
payment, but he does not find out what that means until
he goes to collect it. For all this timber which goes away
the country at large receives no benefit whatever. Per-
haps the girders and piles for export are being hauled a
long way to deep water. The haulers make their wages,
but the local farmers sell them nothing, for the fodder
used is all imported from wherever the timber is going—
either Maoriland or Victoria. The residents of N.S.W.
northern districts have for years been agitating for a line
of railway along the coast, and perhaps in a few years the
line will be sanctioned ; but if the. export of timber con-
tinues, where will the necessary timber for viaducts, &c.,
come from ? Don’t think the large timber reserves will
provide it. I am well acquainted with several of these,
and can say there is very little good timber left there to-
day. There is a little retail planting, but the denudation
is wholesale. For obvious reasons N.S.W. should husband
its timber ; and if it*is to be given away in one sense, why
not appoint the police as assistant-foresters and there
would be more chance of the license-fees being paid ?
Fixing the Capital.
That fatally-fluent politician, Minister Car-
rut hers, of N.S.W., announces that he is in
favor of “fixing the capital at Sydney or in
some portion of New South Wales to be created
a federal territory.” It would be interesting
to know if Minister C. has any distinct idea of
the consequences of his proposition. On one
point there is practically no doubt; the Federal
capital must be Federal territory. The Com-
monwealth Government can’t be merely a lodger
on sufferance in the house of one of the sub-
ordinate States, liable to be ordered off the
grass by the provincial legislature, or to be
ejected bodily if the local authority and it
come to loggerheads, and dependent on the
local Parliament for a place where it can sit
down to get its second wind. It must have a
location of its own—a place where it can plant
itself and its Government offices and its Federal
courts and its arsenals and other pomps
and necessaries without asking permission
of anybody ; in short, its capital must be its
own territory—not merely a place where a
provincial legislature kindly takes it in out of
the wet and allows it to remain as a sort of
adopted orphan. But this is just the reason
why the Commonwealth should select a site
and grow its own capital. If Mr. Carruthers’
plan of making Sydney the capital and creating
it a Federal district is carried out the result
will be that N.S.W. will lose its one great city
altogether. And without it the “ mother
province ” will be a place of comparatively
small importance with a population of about
800,000. It will be the second instead
of the first province of the Federation, Victoria
being far ahead of it. If Maoriland joins in, as
it probably will do before long so as to get on
the right side of the Federal tariff barrier,
then there is every chance that N.S.W. will
presently be only the third State of the
Commonwealth. It will lose, roughly speak-
ing, about one-third of its revenue, possibly
more. If Melbourne became the capital, and
was created a Federal district in like manner,
Victoria would be reduced in one act to a level
very little higher than that of South Australia.
The moral is that the province which tries to
make its one big city the Federal Capital, with
the almost certain result that it will lose its
big city, in a provincial sense, altogether, has
found a first-class way of committing suicide,
and the people who are grabbing so hard after
an empty distinction should think seriously over
this.
Bush. Morality.
Ed. Bulletin, —The conviction of the Dederer
lads, followed by the Cassilis mystery, has
raised a chorus in the press on the enormities
of bush degradation, and foreign missionaries
are bidden with a loud voice to stay at home
and mind the shop. On such data, the con-
clusion and inference are alike illogical and un-
justified. That there are low mental types in
the bush is true enough ; but no one has shown
that they exceed in number those of the towns.
Grapes are not gathered off thorns, nor can
you dump down into a colony, for a generation,
ship-loads of criminals and expect the progeny
to be wholly unlike their progenitors. Any
breeder of live-stock knows more than that.
And the inference, “ that religious instruction
would alter the conformation of human
craniums,” is as rational as the hope of making
racehorses by reading Bible lessons to a mob of
brumbies. Religion and morality have no more
connection than, say, religion and teetotalism.
Devotion naturally arose from man’s first won-
der at the mysteries that surrounded him ; his
morals come from social necessity, and are
being evolved and modified every day from the
same cause. Some of the most bloodthirsty
Italian brigands have been pious, giving large
contributions to the church ; instances are
common in our own police-courts of devout
church-members guilty of fraud and embezzle-
ment, and we have, in Norfolk Island, an entire
community suckled and spoon-fed for genera-
tions on Bible and hymn-book, who have be-
come so depraved as to require special policq
and magisterial regulation. The error is
common of regarding piety unaccompanied by
virtue as humbug. It is true good morals often
go with religion ; so does temperance ; so do
top-hats and many other things ; but the de-
votional and the moral instincts are so wholly
separate that there is absolutely no reason why
the man who lies and defrauds should not have
an honest desire for prayers and hymns and
exhortation, and there is no proof that he prays
and sings and exhorts merely to disguise
his iniquities. There is no reason why he
should not even commit murder on Wednes-
day and yet retain all his genuine love for
Sunday devotions. The idea that devotions are
a remedy for crime is based on the idea that
the two things can’t go together, whereas they
can and often do. It is also taken for granted
that the isolation of the bush tends to de-
pravity. It might with equal reason be said
to lead to asceticism, religious mania and
hysteria, for examples are common in each
direction. And Heaven itself would backslide
occasionally if its angels arrived there in chains
and leg-irons, aud the wonder is, in some of our
districts, not that criminals crop up from time
to time, but that, considering the seed sown,
criminals should be so rare. —H.F.
Premier Reid’s proposal that in the Federal
Senate one-half the members shall retire every
three years is a scheme for the perpetuation of
one of the very worst features of the Victorian
House of 48 Rich Landlords, which is about
the worst House in Australasia. By Reid’s
proposal the Federal Upper House would never
represent the popular sentiment of the day,
and it would never even agree in representing
the popular sentiment of the same yesterday.
At the best, half of it would represent the
feeling of the moment, and the other half that
of three years back ; at the worst half of it
would represent how the electors felt two years
and 11 months ago, and the other half how they
felt five years and 11 months ago. If the
country underwent a great revulsion of feeling
it couldn’t bring the Senate into line with it,
therefore it would lose interest in its Senate.
It could only have half an election at once, and
it doesn’t care for half an election anymore than
it wants to whistle to half a dog. And the
election, instead of coming by the ordinary
process of dissolution when the crisis was in
full blast and public interest was aroused,
would come at a set date every three years,
generally when there was a Sabbath calm in
politics and nothing was moving. The House
which has half or one-third or some other
fraction of an election every three or five or
more years is always one w'here public interest
is about nil, and where the same old fossils
come back practically unopposed year in and
year out.
“ F.” to The Bulletin
Brunton Stephens’ amusing skit on the A.N.A. may or
may not have serious motives behind it. Of course, put
baldly, the limitation of the title “Australian” to the
native-born is absurd —and, similarly, the claims of an
exclusive association to national character are open to the
jibe of wits. But there is practical sense behind the
limitation. (I cannot imagine how the Brunton Stephens
“hard case” could be legislated for.) The A.N.A. is a
product of Victoria, where the average age of the native-
born is comparatively low. A benefit society of the
native-born, therefore, means, in Victoria, a society of
“ good lives.” In some of the branches (there
are 130) the balance-sheets show accumulations of
sick and burial funds that are quite pathetic—for no one
ever dies or demands sick pay. The provision of “ bene-
fits ”is the fly-wheel of the A.N.A. Other organisations,
by scores, established for literary and political purposes,
have gone under. But in Vic. to-day the A.N.A. has
about 10,000 members and £56,000 of funds. It encour-
ages political and literary discussion—and for many years
has always had its representatives in Parliament. It is
not ideal. It runs a rather objectionable sort of lottery,
the motives being intermediate. It harbors a few cads
and boodlers —what big concern does not? But, after all,
the figures show that the number of eligibles to the
A.N.A., other than cads and boodlers, is not so very
small. At the 1891 census the native born in N.S.W.
comprised 72 per cent, of the whole; in Vic., 69 per
cent. ; in Queensland, 52 per cent. The average for Aus-
tralasia was 67 per cent. The percentage of “young
men ” who are native born must be very high. After all,
the A.N.A. is not so very limited—the extraordinary case
quoted by Mr. Brunton Stephens notwithstanding. Are
we not told of a similar case in history—where Queen
Victoria’s mother crossed the channel to England, so
that the “mere expectancy” might be English? I am
afraid Mr. Stephens will think I am a humorless person.
That great statesman, William M‘Millan, of
N.S.W., defines his position on an important
Federal question :
You cannot hand over to the Federal Government the
responsibility and control of the national debts, without
at the same time handing over the railways, which are
the most important asset. This, to my mind, is clear.
The due payment of interest depends entirely on the
economical and efficient management of the railways,
and no practical Government would allow itself to be re-
sponsible for the results of a business over which it had
actually no control There is undoubtedly
a great danger arising out of the local control of
railways, unless provisions, very difficult to frame, are
adopted as a part of the Federal Constitution. While
Freetrade between all the parts, as far as Customs duties
are concerned, might prevail, still by a system of bonuses
or differential rates, the railways might be used as a
practical protection of the commerce of one State against
another. . . . lam therefore opposed to any attempt
to include the absorption of the railway system in the
original constitution.
M'Millan, the Freetrader, is certain that the
railways ought to be federated. Also, he
recognises that if they are not federated it will
mean covert Protection. Likewise, he is in
favor of them not being federated. The little
tinkling importer believes in compelling the
N.S.W. trade to come to Sydney right
up against the door of his own big ware-
house. The blessed principle that trade should
be free and untrammelled may go hang except
when it means that trade should be free to go
to the city where M‘Millan’s warehouse is.
A Broken Hill correspondent to The Bulle-
tin :
It’s a bad thing when a law is passed whose operation
does not extend to the city : if Sydney suffered under the
N.S.W. Municipalities Act that mass of uncertainties and
imperfections would long ago have been superseded.
However, as the “ educated proletariat” in the country
is starting to pick holes in it and creep through them, re-
form will probably be hastened. The voting power is un-
certain. In Sydney both owner and occupier may vote ;
under the Municipalities Act “ occupier, owner and
lessee” may be enrolled; but only the one “who pays
the rates ” may vote. Still, as nobody can say for certain
when an enrolled person presents himself whether he has
paid the rates or not, he has to sign a declara-
tion setting out that he is a “ ratepayer ” in
respect of some particular premises ; and then there
is no power that can prevent the man voting ; so
the Full Court held in the Pte case. If he isn’t a rate-
payer, but has declared that he is, of course it is perjury.
During the last year or two the Labor-party in the bigger
provincial centres has been extending operations by
running municipal candidates, with the result, in some
cases, that owners and occupiers, landlords and tenants,
Conservatives and Labor men, have come in conflict. Not
often, though, has the tenant thought it worth while to
rush off wildly and pay up rates in order to get his single
vote ; so the landlord has done it instead—only more
leisurely. But now the “educated proletariat” has
started to argue the thing out. “ I pay the rent,” he
says ; “ old Jones has got nothing except rent; therefore,
he must pay the rates out of the rent. So I pay
the rates to him in instalments, and he hands
the money in at the office—except when he sticks to it, in
which case the council collars my furniture. Wherefore
I am the ratepayer.” Accordingly, having thus squared
his conscience, he proceeds to vote. But then, the land-
lord comes along and votes, too ; and the Act says that
only one shall vote. Things are therefore mixed, and
threaten to become still more mixed—unless a chance
prosecution straightens them out. At present, each side
is calling the other a shameless perjurer; but neither is
apparently quite sure enough about his own position to
sool the law on to the other.
“ H.” lo The Bulletin
Without a doubt, Q. party politics are shaking under-
neath. There is not only an apparent—some leaders say it
is only “ apparent” and that differences have been healed
—slump among Labor members, but the heterogeneous
conglomerate of the Ministerial phalanx is decomposing
into antagonistic atoms. Premier Nelson wants to quit.
Ten “ Ministerialists ” are named who are said to be will-
in" to coalesce with some of the Labor members—some
Labor members barred. The “ political association that
ran the elections for Nelson is, for all party purposes, as
dead as a moonstruck codfish. The P.U. and the
Federated Employers’ Association were known to have
put up a big purse for last election’s purposes. The
amount of this is variously stated, but the favorite
sum named is £12,000. This is said to have gone
slap-bang into the treasury of the Bolcock agency
and is further declared to have vanished—appar-
etdlv nnAly in “circulars.” The P.U. and the
F.L.A., “c., are " full up” of paying “subscriptions” to
a vanishing fund, and their present political attitude is
apathy. Now is the real time when the workers should
organise—ay, organise and cabal, if they see a road-open-
ing. Tozer is declared to be willing to buy them —poli-
tically buy them, of course. Question; Can the workers
buy up To/.kr —also, politically, of course ? Can they
afford to do it? Nelson is still in the “never mind"’
mood as regards everything. He is a master of laisaer
faire. And his own feeling really is—“ Never mind. If
you insist on me remaining Premier, well —mine’s whisky
and never mind. If you’ll let me go, well—never mind,
mine’s whisky.” Secret negotiations are at work, and
Tozer’S visit to Sydney was not altogether unconnected
with them.

H.G. (Maryborough): Received Odin : Sentiment

Place the foot upon a sheet of paper and
draw a pencil closely round it for correct
shape. Measure with a tape around the
foot as indicated, drawing it medium tight
giving inches and fraction of inches ; mea-
suring over a tight stocking.
The measurement “top’' is for ladies’
boots.
If possible send with measurements a
sample boot, that has been worn, as a
check.
Select shape required as per diagrams,
and state whether soles are to be light,
medium, or heavy (for winter).

Her Majesty’s (Sydney) groaned in every
tier beneath the crowd which rolled up on Satur-
day night to look on at the revival of the “ Silver
King.” That fine old play had a memorable
night out, and even in its newest days it couldn’t
have been much better reoeived, or much better
played. Scot Inglis, in The Bulletin’s opinion,
is the best Silver King Australia has yet seen;
even with silver at 2s. 6d. an ounce he lays over
all competitors. His youth gives him an advan-
tage even over Titheradge, who might otherwise be
ahead, and he leaves the rest of the dynasty of
the silver kings behind on general principles.
Inglis is at his best in Act 1, where Wilfred
Denver is an intoxicated dude going downhill in
a top hat to perdition. He is boozed with a
gentlemanly and realistic and experienced drunk;
even in his maddest zig-zags his Denver is
a picturesque character with vine-leaves in his
hair—not simply with beer in his feet, which is
quite a different thing. He is almost equally
brilliant in Denver’s other moods and circum-
stances, and, taken as a whole, the latest silver
king will keep ahead of most competitors. Some-
body presented a vast wreath to him when he
returned from Nevada, and he took it on his stick
and carried it away, with the bitter expression of
one who intended jumping on the vegetable as
soon as he got out of sight. Rumor has it that
Scot Inglis objects to be decked with the girly
garland, and promptly boots it into next
century when it is inflicted upon him.
» « * *
Miss Harrie Ireland, though not quite the equal
of Henrietta Watson, is above the average as Nellie
Denver; and Harry Roberts fills the part of
Jaikes quite as well as that weary servitor
deserves. _ The aged retainer is always very much
the same.’ Denver alters greatly ; various actors
have made his age vary 40 years or more, and his
bulk ranges from 10 to 17 stone, but Dan’l
Jaikes is cast in a fixed mould, and his twinkling
legs are always about the same thickness, and the
back-view of him looks like the same inky cock-
roach all the time. Roberts is as good a Jaikes as
any other, and better than a great many. Cecil
Ward’s Spider is a new rendering. He is smaller
and milder than most of them, and treats his
accomplices not forcibly, but with a small voice
and a culchawed sneer. Barring a rather weak
and ineffective Father Christmas, the present co.
is about the best all-round combination which has
tackled the “Silver King” in Sydney. Little
Daisy Ireland deserves a special mention all to
herself, as a chi Id-actress who plays cleverly, and
doesn’t look at all like the usual laborious and
scared stage infant, and various other people who
also deserve mention are unavoidably held over.
* # # * C
For reasons that are about as indistinct as
Freeman Kitchen’s letters on Federation, “ Cheer,
Boys, Cheer,” comes to an untimely end this
week, after a season of 12 nights only, and that
great, gilded phantasmagoria, the “ Lights of
London,” rises out of its grave to put its ghostly
face in the Royal bill for Saturday. Why this
literary mammoth should come from its resting-
place now more than at any other time to gambol
in 187 acts beneath the moon isn’t especially evi-
dent ; probably it is because the Rignold season is
near its end, and the overgrown drama feels called
upon to lumber across the stage at least once,
merely as a matter of custom, before the end.
The Bulletin will view the departure of
“ Cheer, Boys,” with regret for several reasons,
but mainly for the sake of Miss Emily
Hughes, whose bright and breezy little widow
is a character to be viewed often and fallen
in love with violently. When that lady
rises up in defence of the slandered heroine and
denounces a room-full of the British aristocracy,
and when she desires to publicly belt Plimmer
with her umbrella, and, later on, when the
heroine’s character is all gone, and her defender
rises up and denounces the universe in general on
her behalf, and offers to belt the entire solar sys-
tem, she carries most of the drama on her two bare
shoulders, and carries it remarkably well. If
arrangements could be made for her presence in
the battle-scene, where she could hold the fort in
evening-dress with her parasol when everybody
else was down, the result would be simply over-
whelming. The Bulletin presents a portrait of
Miss Hughes on page 11 of this issue, partly in
consideration of her ability and partly because she
looks better than the portrait of the remarkable
oldest inhabitant, aged 105, and still in possession
of all his faculties, and able to distinctly remember
the time when he saw George Thornton receiving
Capt. Cook on Manly pier.
* * * *
Frank Thornton is due at Sydney Criterion
on Saturday with the “ Strange Adventures of
Miss Brown.” That unusual female arrives with
strong recommendations from her last place in
Melbourne, and on the strength of these and
many previous testimonials she proudly puts down
her band-box and her goloshes in the hall of the
Btrange city, and leaves her boots out to be cleaned
wfth perfect confidence. Lohr acts as manager,
and is generally responsible for the lady’s safety
and well-being. The Brough Comedy Co. leaves
for Melbourne to fill Thornton’s vacant place at
the Bijou.
** * *
The announcement that Harry Rickards
had taken over Sydney Palace, vice Goatcher,
was only what everybody expected. The
fate of every variety house is obviously to
fall into the hands of the absorbent
Rickards sooner or later. The Palace and the
Tivoli companies are now getting mixed up and
re-assorted. “My Fancy,” the moderately sur-
prising dancer who formerly did her respectably
ordinary dance at the new house, has shifted along
to the old one, while Edgar Granville, Amy Dew-
hurst the “top-boot dancer,” and ventriloquist
Winton have been turned on at the Palace. The
decease of the pantomime having released Crackles
and Lawton, the latter is staged at the Palace,
and the former at the Tivoli. Charles Cogill and
Florrie Forde are at both houses simultaneously,
and the Amazonian ballet has been transferred to
the new venture, and the agile Manager Leete is
in both places at once. Otherwise the chief dif-
ference between them is that one gallery is 6d.
and the other Is.
** * *
Howells and Stevens have decided to
knock off concert business for a while, and in an-
nouncing the fact that they have had more than
enough bad luck to go on with, Howells was rough
on “musical Adelaide,” which used, as a rule, to
get first hearing of the imported artists. Santley
and Cyril Tyler showed a profit in Adelaide, but
all other companies engaged of late years were a
financial failure until they got along to Melbourne
and Sydney, where in some cases the Adelaide loss
was recovered (and yet the advance notices from
S.A. invariably told of enthusiastic receptions
from crowded audiences). Mr. Howells is very
sore, and with reason, on the subject of Mark
Hambourg’s cool treatment by the southern saints.
Mark’s weird, peculiar playing was a matter of
taste, but nobody could fairly deny his genius for
administering systematic punishment to the piano.
Adelaide set the unlovely youth down as a mere
“thumper” when he was, in good truth, a hurri-
cane fighter, and a scientist at that.
This is the last week, bar one, of “ Matsa”
at Melbourne Princess’s, although the demand for
that imposing spectacle remains healthy. After a
turn in the provinces, “Matsa” will arrive in
Sydney on Feb. 27, by which time, perhaps, sundry
members of the company will have been put
through a judicious course of galvanic baths, and
tonics taken internally. The Bulletin herewith
begs to mention that anything which is worth
doing at all is worth doing well, and if the leading
“ Matsa ” offenders don’t take the aged maxim to
heart when they make their new start, they may
expect to hear of something to their disadvantage
from several quarters.
* * • *
At the end of the “ Matsa ” run in Sydney
the Firm will organise a big specialty company,
including most of the people from their two pan-
tomimes, reinforced by importations from ’Ome.
The intention is to tour Australia with this new
arrangement of forces, making a particular feature
of the ballet. All the best dancing material at
W. & M.’s command is to be gathered together
into a ballet that shall surpass even the Blue
Ballet of three years ago, and leave nothing to be
desired by the most exacting and bald-headed
critic of the female leg divine.

Preparations for “The Prisoner of Zenda,”
which follows the panto, at Melb. Princess’s on
Feb. 13, are on something beyond the usual ad-
vertisement scale of splendor. Dresses alone
will cost <£lsoo or thereabouts, and by way of
convincing the public that there is no deception in
the matter, a collection of beauteous bodices, and
skirts guaranteed to trail six feet along the stage,
is now being exhibited at a local draper’s.
Also, a choice assortment of sassiety orna-
ments —Mrs. Maesmore MorriSfMiss Sutton, &c.
who have undertaken to wear purple and fine linen
in the forthcoming production, may be often seen
doing the Block in the meantime as a guarantee ot
good faith. For the parts that require acting
rather than raiment a company of seven has just
arrived —Miss Ada Ferrar the leading lady,
angelic Miss Elliot Page, Miss Mortyne, Messrs.
Hawtry, Gaston Mervale, Carvill and Julius
Knight, which latter was here some seven years
ago. “ The Prisoner of Zenda” promises to be an
Event.

An abnormal array of talent at the Opera
House continues to earn a due reward at the pay-
boxes, but the poor old Haleck has relapsed into
its time-honored state of desolation and darkness.
“ For England’’—jingo libel on the apathetic
Boer—
We sing thy scenic glories, though we’ve sung ’em
all before,
The foxhunt (so exciting that the dogs forget to
bark
When they trot from Little Bourke-street through
the glade in Cranbourne Park)
Is still a great attraction, and a never-failing
source
Of pleasure for disciples of the ’urdle-jumpin’
’orse ;
The moonlit Abbey Ruins, where the villain finds
a way
To shift the angry parent of the girl who went
astray ;
The Rocky Pass at Somewhere, and the Strong-
hold of the Foe,
And the dauntless British forces laying Dutch-
men’s courage low ;
The welcoming of Baker to the home where he
was born,
(Which marks the wicked majah, who is laughing
him to scorn,
And pouring forth the contents of his bitter,
wrathy vials
Till a Zulu money-lender comes along from Seven
Dials
To charge him with a murder, and arrest him for
a crime
That somehow didn’t get him into trouble at the
time).
Those scenes are well deserving of applause in any
case,
So we sing again their praises for the sake of filling
space.
At Poverty Point.
The Mrs. Charles Charrington, whose name was men-
tioned in a t’other-day cablegram as that of the co-re-
spondent in the Earl Cowley divorce proceedings, has been
wrongly confounded with the lady of that name, at whose
representation of “Camille” the hard-swearing, ex-
bullock-punching, beknighted Palmer, President of tbe
Q. Legislative Council, and at that time Acting-Governor
of Q., refused to be present. “ Bully ” Palmer felt him-
self too good, too immature, and too unsafe to risk attend-
ing such a show.
A medical correspondent re Gabriel, the black who has
turned white “ All whitenings of the skin are due to
congenital absence of pigment, as in the albino, or to
later loss of pigment, as in such cases as this present
freak. Such loss appears to be always of nervous origin.
The whitening may be patchy or general; anaesthetic
lepers invariably whiten. Kipling speaks of the ‘ silver
man.’ Gabriel might well be examined by a leprosy ex-
pert with a view to public health.”

“ Other day I passed a George-street, Sydney, show*

bition, and a piano jerked out the ‘ Dead March ’ unceas-
ingly. It struck me that, should something happen—
but the public revels in its funeral.” —T.D.
Amy Sherwin and Hugo Gorlitz, before leaving South
Africa, were favored with an interview with President
Kruger. The old Dutchman, who is a connoisseur in
pretty faces, is said to have kissed Am - on both cheeks,
much to the discomfiture of Gorlitz, while Madame
Kruger witnessed the scene with phlegmatic apathy.
Legal proceedings have already been begun by some of
the people involved in the Sydney Palace-L\ceum em-
broglio, with a view to having certain incidents connected
with the business arrangements of the parties cleared up
by the courts.
The finale to the Goatcher season at Sydney Lyceum
occurred last Monday, when the effects of the manage-
ment were auctioned under distraint for rent. Proceed-
ings opened with a lively set-to between two robust
gentlemen—whose interests clashed —during which much
sparring and tinted language was indulged in by the
belligerents. After these warlike persons were repressed
the assets of the fallen manager—mostly scenery—were
brought to the hammer and sold for £47.
Nelly Mortvne returns in the “ Prisoner of Zenda ” Co.,
with astonishing prestige. Her name, we conclude
assumed, is a reminder of that Ellen Mortyn about whom
Dr. Neild could write such a romance. She came to
Melbourne under engagement to Brooke, and, after
greatly delighting the public, died mysteriously A post-
mortem dispelled wicked slanders circulated.
One of Chief Justice Madden’s daughters performs the
late Sadie Macdonald’s song and dance, “ Baby isn’t old
enough to know ’’—possibly out of compliment to a cer-
tain bereaved lordship.
George E. Howard, thrown from a horse and killed
almost instantaneously, was Melbourne’s leading ’celloist,
and as a teacher was locally unrivalled; several of his
pupils will probably gain prominence as violinists.
The Howard Conservatoire of Music, just beginning to
bloom, will now probably die with the founder, and Mar-
shall Hall will reign supreme.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Does it ‘ enchant ’to see a child,
at a time and age when she ought to be in bed, posturing
on a music-hall stage? And does ‘ intelligence ’ manifest
itself in individual originality, or the faithful reproduc-
tion of others’ originality? If the latter, way for the infant
prodigy. Alma Grey does not ‘ sing suggestive songs.’
She does not. She sang, ‘ Please buy me one like papa
had yesterday,’ at the Tivoli a while ago. But that is a
song the daughter of the day might take even her mother
to hear. It is the touching and simple chronicle of how
* papa ’ kissed the cook.” —E.
Writes someone: “‘The play’s the thing!’ is it? In
* Under the Czar,’ by a monetarily successful English
play-carpenter, the sensation is a man being hanged with
proper realism. This same builder has a new piece
‘ Right or Wrong,’ going now, in which the villain decoys
the hero to sit, with the heroine on his knee, in a faked
chair, which is really the seat of an electrocution ap-
paratus. By a machine accident the current is deviated
on to the villain himself, who squirms epileptically all
over the stage with burnt and distorted features. ‘ The
Serpent’s Coil ’ now retires into obscurity. Presently,
doubtless, Haleck patrons will rejoice to watch a baby
being eaten piecemeal by pigs and ravenous rats, or a
villain butcher made into pemmican—buttons, apron
and all—in his own sausage-machine, or a real dissecting-
room, wherein real medical students with real knives will
dissect the perished remains of Charlie Kingston, under
the supervision of Georgy Reid’s own eyeglass, while Pea-
cock sits up on the cornice, and cachinnates hideously
an accompaniment to a pibroch by the awful M‘lntyre in
philabeg and hose.”
