A stray American paper, apropos Mrs. Stowe, exhumes
the incident of Beecher selling- a lovely octoroon girl, aged
about 19, in his New York church, by auction, on Sunday
morning, before a packed fashionable and excited congre-
gation This was shortly before Boucicault wrote the
“Octoroon,”and furnished him with the mostthrillinginci-
dent. Having escaped from slavery, the girl was detained
at Washington, but allowed to go on parole to New York.
She was very religious, and undertook to go South again,
if not redeemed. Such was the confidence in her word
that the M'Closkey of this real drama gave 1200dol. for
her, but offered to let her go on being recouped. Beecher
stood her up before his congregation, and raised the
money.
A shark, 27ft. 6in. long and 15ft. in girth,
has been caught off the Maoriland coast. It
made a vicious attack on a fishing - boat, and
was with difficulty beaten off. It took 30 men to
hold it after it was hooked.
James Ashton, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), pub-
lishes his Federal election address in this issue.
Mr. Ashton, judged by his address and speeches,
is one of the straightest, most thorough-going,
most Australian, and least parochial of the candi-
dates announced up to date, and though The
Bulletin disagrees on one or two points, it
regards him as about the most promising man
who has yet come forward.
Speaker Joseph Abbott, of N.S.W., an-
nounces himself as a candidate for the Federal
Convention. His manifesto contains no state-
ment of his views on any single point, so the
people are invited to take him on trust, on the
grounds that he has been an M.P. for 17 years,
neaker for seven years, is a big boss Mason, head
man of the A.M.P. Society, and is 6ft. or
thereabouts in height. And The Bulletin’s
opinion is that even a man 26ft. 4|in. in height
shouldn’t be taken blindly in this fashion. There-
fore, it waits to hear something definite about the
lordly Abbott’s views.
What the Yictorian Conservative party
gigglingly hold up their sleeve, under the im-
pression that it is a trump-card, is the running of
Duncan Gillies against Geo. Turner for St. Kilda
at next general election. The rosy-cheeked ex-
Agent-General would stand the chance of the
proverbial Buckley, though he could be easily
persuaded into a contest. At last St. Kilda fight,
barrister Fatman Coldham had the promises of
three-fourths of the electors, yet when the numbers
went up Coldham said in his speech that there
were more liars in St. Kilda than in any other
constituency in the universe.
Apropos L. F. Heydon’s proposals that, if
there must be a federation, which he strongly
objects to in any case, it shall only be for ten
years, so that N.S.W. can withdraw at the end
of that time if it wants to, instead of being
forcibly retained by the brute majority of the
other provinces. Supposing that Riverina or the
Barrier didn’t want to withdraw while Sydney
did, what is the difference between them being
forcibly dragged out by Sydney, or Sydney being
forcibly kept in by the other provinces ? Heydon
makes no provision for such a case. His provin-
cial idea of freedom, apparently, is only freedom
for the county of Cumberland to refuse the rest
of the province any freedom whatever.
Another collision at Redfern railway-
station, Sydney, arising in exactly the same
fashion as that of two years ago. One train was
coming in ; another was going out, and the latter
smashed into the first carriage of the train ap-
proaching. It tumbled over slowly, the roof came
off, and out fell the passengers higgledy-piggledy
—for all the world like the animals from an over-
turned Noah’s-ark. Luckily, neither train was
going at speed, and a few bruises were all the in-
juries—or all, at least, which the railway people
acknowledged to the reporters ; for Sydney rail-
way people are shrewd in their generation. The
accident draws renewed attention to the in-
adequacy of Redfern station for the traffic. If
Govt, does not move, to the cost of the new
station may any day be added <£loo,ooo damages
for a really big collision—the personal responsi-
bility for which will be not with the Commis-
sioners, but with the Ministry.
Phil May is now among the most-inter-
viewed of “ lions,” and many and various are the
yarns ascribed to him. May loves a joke, and the
temptation to draw the long-bow must be extreme
when still another scribe runs him to earth. But,
despite his exceptional popularity and success, May
is modest and generous, and would never have
sanctioned such a misleading yarn as was
published in a recent number of San Francisco
Argonaut, which stated that : “ He went to Aus-
tralia, and by his efforts raised the Sydney Bulle-
tin from an obscure paper to a wide circulation.
He then returned to London with a reputation.”
Little exception can be taken to the latter sentence,
but the first part is unjust and untrue. Several
years before Mr. May’s engagement, the “obscure”
paper had become such a pronounced success that
a representative visited America and England to
secure the services of a first-class artist, and had
the good fortune to engage Mr. Livingston
Hopkins, a caricaturist of top-rank in that city
of brilliant cartoonists —New York. With such
an accession the paper continued to increase in
circulation and popularity, and when it was
decided to further add to the strength of the
artistic staff, the then young and comparatively
unknown Phil. May was engaged, with the result!
that after four years he returned to London with
a much-enhanced reputation. No one has better
earned his great artistic fame than Phil,
May, and those who know him best know him to
be incapable of making or conveying such a boast
as has been attributed to him by the paragraph in
question.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The A.J.S. Bank’s balance-sheet is a tale of
woeful mishap. The loss for the half-year is £48,200.
Even the Commercial of Australia, in the very bad half-
year before its second reconstruction, only managed to
accumulate an avowed loss of £24,000, and the City of
Melbourne’s best effort —the one which broke it—was
under £20,000, so the A. J.S. has put up the record loss of
Australia in recent years. Last half-year it was announced
that “ the operations of the bank showed a net profit of
£23,264,” and though The Bulletin suggested, reading
between the lines, that this was really made by selling
the bank’s large holding of Consols and realising a big
premium t'hereon, nothing was said about it in the
balance-sheet at the time. Now it is acknowledged that
the profit was obtained in the way this paper suggested,
and that the ordinary “ operations of the bank ” showed
a loss of £B4oo—which is a very different impression from
that conveyed six months ago. For the half ended Deo.,
’95, the profit was returned at £25,825, so the slump in one
year (£74,000) is awful. Even this doesn’t represent the
whole trouble. A year ago there still remained £573,000
of the old bank’s reserves to cover depreciation in the
old assets. Six months ago there remained only £374,000,
go it appears that £199,000 went in depreciation in one
half-year. Now the remainder—how much it is doesn’t
appear—is said to be carried to a contingency account,
but as no contingency account appears among the
liabilities, this is probably much the same as a complete
writing off. If this is so, any further losses must come
out of the profits of the new bank —and the new bank has
a £48,000 loss just now instead of a profit. Considering
that the 4J per cent, deposits have been reduced during
the year by over £1,300,000, and a very large saving in
interest thus effected, even the low interest rates prevail-
ing hardly account for the drop, especially as most other
banks are showing a more or less marked improvement
just now.
The A. J.S. is now negotiating in England for a
reduction of interest on the remaining five millions of its
4$ per cent, deposits. But even if it gets a reduction to
3 per cent, the saving will be only £75,000 a year, while
the gap to be filled to make ends meet on the present
basis of things is £96,400 a year. Even a reduction to 2(
per cent., which looks too good to be hoped for, would
only, at the present rate, leave a margin of £3600 to cover
depreciation of the old assets—that depreciation which
apparently swallowed £199,000 in a single half-year. And
the board’s statement, that it “ continues to receive
advices which are considered favorable for a reduction of
the rate of interest ” doesn’t sound especially sanguine.
The business for the half-year shows an increase in note
circulation and a small reduction in current accounts. Cash
on hand is much the same, but cash and other liquid
assets combined (£1,374,468) are £58,000 better. There is
nothing very striking or definite about any of these
figures. The latest recorded sales of the shares (£7 10s.
paid up) were at the very humble figure of 15s. The
big item, “ bills receivable, bills discounted, and all
other debts and other assets, £7,139,357,” ought to be
interesting reading if the Victorian Companies Act
was in force in N.S.W., and it was necessary to show how
much of the mass consisted of foreclosed property, and
how much had been written off it for depreciation. The
present signs are that a great deal depends on the
negotiation now proceeding with the reconstructed
depositors, and if it doesn’t come off, or only comes off
on a very small scale, it looks as if the position might be
very serious indeed. Under all the circumstances, the
4 and 5 per cent, dividends which the A.J.S. paid so
gaily until a year ago look like very reckless financing.

offer was 11s. 1

The balance-sheet of the Queensland National
Bank is a disastrous and hopeless-looking document.
The half-year’s takings don’t cover working ex-
penses, and the loss is £1607. Last half there was
an alleged profit of £10,021, and the one previous a
dubious profit of £13,130, so the drop is something very
large. It is apparently brought about by the fact that
the recent Parliamentary investigation shut down, in part
at least, on the practice of showing profits by charging in-
terest which couldn’t be recovered upon advances that
were practically dead. During the half-year the cash and
State notes on hand at head office and all branches have
dropped from £1,998,000 to £838,000, and the total liquid,
or allegedly liquid, assets from £2,132,000 to £1,403,000.
This is a truly awful falling away. The “ deposits and
other liabilities” have diminished nearly £900,000, and, as
the Q.N. B. hasn’t released any of its reconstructed deposits,
it would seem that the public or the Government, or both,
have been rushing to get their loose money out of the ill-
fated institution, and that this is the cause of the reduc-
tion. There is a very small reduction in the advances,
possibly because the Q.N.B.’s advances are not of a kind
that can be easily realised, so the diminution in the
deposits is almos,t all represented by the fall of the liquid
assets. If the rush had continued at the same rate much
longer, and the. Nelson Government hadn’t come to the
rescue with a temporary State guarantee, it is interesting
to speeulate as to where the Q.N.B. would have gone to.
The auditors’ certificate is a bitter irony. A few
weeks back an independent investigation showed that
about £2,500,000 of the alleged assets were not assets at
all, but losses. Yet they appear in the balance-sheet
under the same old heading as actual property, and the
same two auditors certify in exactly the same old terms to
the correctness of the sheet which the bank itself acknow-
ledges to be a lie. That is to say, they state that “ it is
a full and fair one, exhibiting a true statement of the
affairs of the bank as shown by the books." The exact
value of that kind of auditors’-certificate is evident when
it is seen that it fits in just as well when the assets are
proved to be largely bogus as it did when they were still
professing to be good. If the new Victorian Act, under
which the manager, auditors, and two directors have to
swear that, to the very best of their belief, the balance-
sheet is a correct statement of affairs as they are, applied
in Queensland, either the Q.N.B. would have had its
revelations long ago, or a pretty comprehensive criminal
trial would be looming, or a lot more people would be
dead. A law which makes everybody concerned either
tell the truth or commit perjury in the broadest terms,
with a large gaol looming in front, has a lot of points
about it.
* * * *
Some of the old Q. N. Bank directors have the
impudence to seek re-election, though the commission
report, if it did not convict them of knowingly paying
dividends to themselves from the funds of an insolvent
institution, certainly did convict them of gross incapacity.
* • * *
The English, Scottish, and Australian Bank re-
ports a loss for the 12 months ended 30th June, 1896, of
£24,576. The 1895 loss was over £16,000, so things have
become a good deal worse within the 12 months. In
1894 there was a profit of £10,153, therefore the E.S.
and A. has gone backward considerably for the past
two years. Since June, ’96, however, the new arrange-
ment with the depositors has come into force. Under its
provisions the institution saves £15,000 a year interest
for certain, and can cut down interest in case of necessity
so as to save another £45,000, making £60,000 in all. If
the drop in profits continues at the present rate for two
years more the whole £60,000 relief will be wanted ; but
this seems hardly likely, and according to present appear-
ances the E.S. and A. has now a sufficient margin to
keep it square. The shareholders’ prospects, however,
are about as poor as they well can be ; things
will have to improve £70,000 a year before even 5
per cent, will be visible on the far horizon, and the next
dividend looks many years off. English management, as
shown in the case of the E.S. and A., looks no better
than the ordinary every-day Australian article. In two
years the institution only reduced its reconstructed
deposits by £179,609, which is very little off £4,000,000.
It has a capital of £971,000; no reserve-fund ; an accu-
mulated loss of £34,000 ; a very moderate new business,
and an expense rate which is a lot heavier than that of
the Victoria or Commercial of Australia—both consider-
ably larger concerns. Also, some of its deposits are sell-
ing as low as 12s. in the £, and its shares seem to have no
market-value in particular—in Australia, at all events.
* * m *
The Australian Widows’ Fund presents a mode-
rate-looking report for the year ended 31st October, ’96.
Renewal premiums (£137,518) are nearly £2OOO less than
for the previous year, £BOOO less than for ’94, and £15,000
less than for ’93. New premiums (£13,236) are £l2OO
better than last year, and £3OOO better than ’94 or ’93, so
the total premium income since ’93 has gone down about
£12,000. Interest on investments (£56,536) is £3OOO more
than last year, but £lOOO less than for ’94, despite the fact
that the funds were then much smaller. The rate of
interest earned is now only £4 Bs. per cent, per annum ;
whereas in ’93 it was as high as £5 ss. The total income
(£213,879) is £7OOO better than in ’9s—which is something ;
and £BOOO worse than in ’93—which isn’t very much. The
expense rate is about the worst feature in the Widows’
Fund. It got down, in ’93, to 22 per cent, on premium
income. It ascended to nearly 27 per cent, in ’95, and in
’96 it is only down a very small fraction below 25 per
cent. The “properties acquired by foreclosure” now
represent £61,000 against £24,000 a year ago, so the
society has evidently had none too good luck during the
year. The amount saved and added to the funds is
£68,000, whereas in ’93 it was £lOO,OOO, loss of business by
surrenders and lapsed policies being the chief cause of
the trouble. Also, the quinquennial report shows a very
big writing off to cover depreciation during the last five
years—exact amount not known as the document hasn’t
come to hand, but said to be enough to swallow up most
of the bonus.
The general result is that the Widows’ Fund is
a respectable, decently-managed office with nothing sen-
sational about it, and suffering a good deal from over-
much competition. The assets look good enough,
and there is nothing suspicious to be noted about any of
the items in the balance-sheet. If the Widows’ Fund
could arrange an amalgamation with another office
equally solid and respectable and slow-going, and thereby
greatly reduce the expense account of both of them, it
wouM be a good thing. Too much insurance offiee in
proportion to population is the chief cause of big expense
ratios in Australia, and the big expenses keep bonuses
low—or premiums high, which is practioally the same
thing.

On two consecutive days recently the loans made
by S.A. State Land Bank totalled £4695. Business is
steadily improving, and even the most Tory scoffer of the
crowd has almost ceased scoffing at the Kingston Govern-
ment’s Socialistic experiment. In another year the
chances are that Copley and his crowd will be heard
stating that they advocated the State Land Bank all
along.
* * * *
One of the favorite points of N.S. W. anti-
Federal orators is that their own province pays a lower
rate of interest on its debts than does any of its neigh-
bors, therefore a pooling of liabilities means a loss to
N.S.W. The statement is true enough in a sense, but it
doesn’t necessarily follow, as alleged, that the credit of
the mother province is much better than that of its
neighbors. In 1884 interest dropped from 4 per cent, to
3)4- In 1896 it dropped to 3 per cent. The province
that has borrowed most furiously since 1884 made the
biggest reduction in the average rate of interest over its
whole debt. N.S.W. has borrowed harder since ’B4 than
any other province, and about as hard as any other two
provinces, having added 30 millions to its debt in 12 years,
hence its average interest rate is lowest. If it had bor-
rowed 60 millions at the recent reduced rates it would
have been lower still, and if it had raised 120 millions in
’96 when the 3 per cent, rate came in, its average rate of
interest would be a lot lower again. That is the beginning,
middle and end of the mystery.
* • * *
The Victorian State Land Bank sold its first
£25,000 worth of 3 per cent, bonds in Melbourne at about
£lOO Is. The transaction got through, however, by the
skin of its teeth ; the public only took £21,625, and the
Savings Bank made up the rest. Three per cent, is
cutting things a shade too fine —or at least quite fine
enough. Even J. Bull is not taking any 3 per cent,
securities at a premium with no certainty of long cur-
rency, and the attempt to cut the local investor below
the British one is wholly unjustifiable. When money
gets just a shade less plentiful in Melbourne, the necessity
for making future issues at Z\ will probably become
apparent.
The Southern Insurance Co. (Melbourne) has
apparently struck another patch of disaster. It started
the half-year with a credit to profit and loss of £27,017,
and ended it with only £25,495, so that the loss on six
months work is £1522. Then the co. pays alO per cent,
dividend (£2500), making the total loss £4022. Further,
£5OOO has been taken from reserves to form a fund to
cover depreciation of securities, and all these things com-
bined make a pretty bad slump for one half-year. The
record for four successive half-years is as follows :
(1) The Southern made a loss of £1393, and paid
£2500 dividend on top of it ; (2) The profit
was £1689, and the £2500 dividend paid as usual
converted it into another serious loss ; (3) The profit was
£3432, and after paying £2500 div. there remained £932 ;
(4, present half-year) The loss is £1522, and the £2500
dividend is paid once more, making the loss £4022. Total
for two years, £7794 lost through paying dividends that
were not earned, and £5OOO taken from reserves to cover
depreciation. How long does the board imagine that even
a big and solid co. can go on at this rate ?

The bland enquiry, “Isn’t that sweating?”
made by old Doctor Youl, Melb.’s comic coroner, at
the inquest on the murdered Brady, was strongly to
the point. The head of the firm which had employed
both the deceased and the prisoner was giving evidence.
Brady he said, had been in the employ about 15 years,
and at the time of his death was drawing £3 10s. a week
—for working from 9 to 6 in the daytime, “ going back ’
at night on an average three times a week, and being the
confidential clerk of the concern. Phillips was but a
comparative novice ; he had been in the place only a
trifle of five years or so, and consequently he was getting
but half-a-sovereign a week. The question that the
coroner put was on the tip of everybody’s tongue. The
witness or the firm or somebody evidently felt a little
ashamed over the wages paid, for in the information sup-
plied to the newspapers at the time of Phillips’ arrest
Brady’s earnings were given as £4 10s. and Phillips’ as
17s 6d But when the firm got into the box and had to
depose’ on oath to the facts of the case, the accountant’s
screw fell £1 suddenly, and the youth’s diminished by 37£
per cent. , , , ,

The Australian Trading World is a little
I rind on sheet whose time and space are largely devoted
to the humming of certain W.A. and N.S.W. mines. The
Painbula Gold Mines, Ltd., appears to be a special favorite.

gold, 60oz. 19dwt. ; (B)gold, 620 z. 7dwt. per ton.” The
mining manager has been instructed to “ ship home at
once a couple of tons of this ore.” Why not a couple of
thousand ? Of course, it is proposed to increase the
capital “in order to fully equip and work the mine.”
Holders are advised to stick to their shares, “certainly
not to sell at par or anything near it.”

From Grafton (N.S.W.) : “The escort from
Bucca Creek brought two cakes of gold from the Beacon
mine, but no information was given as to weight. It is
said to be 1600 oz.” The mine is known to be looking re-
markably well —so well that the chances are beginning to
look favorable for the Exploration Co. pulling out well on
the deal, though it will take a lot of gold to do it. A good
example has been shown in the remarkably quick way
the co. got things going.

Amos Gold Mining Co., N.S.W., decided to do

* * * *
Birthday, Parkes (N.S.W.), reef, said to be 7ft.
wide and shows payable gold, upwards of 500 tons of stone
at grass and a 10-head battery nearing completion. The
best confirmation of all this is the share market, the value
of the mine having more than doubled within the last two
months.
* * * *
Mt. Drysdale, N.S.W. : “The Drysdale mill is
still running on better stone than for some time past.”
Faint hope. It would be more to the point to say :
“ There is still a chance of striking in this mine another
wonderfully rich streak, or chimney, of ore, such as sent
the shares up to £BOO before it duffered out. Whereupon
the boom departed, and desolation has reigned in the
camp ever since.”
* * * *
New Bushman’s—Parkes, N.S.W. The chair-
man : “ There was nothing exceedingly rich in sight, but
he had no hesitation in saying that the mine looked
better to-day than it did six months ago. He
thought when the mine got into full swing again they
would not be more than £lOO in debt.” He might also,
in common fairness, have explained that what had been
foreshadowed by the D.T. special mining reporter, was
exactly what had happened, despite his own declarations
to the contrary. A newspaper can tell as many lies as it
likes in favor of a mine, and be applauded for it; but let
it tell the truth against a mine and a howl of discontent
follows, as in this particular instance.

Wentworth mine, N.S.W., owner J. Bull, no
div. last six months, although the gold won for the year
amounted to 16,5240 z. Record for five years —gold,
104,8240 z. ; divs, £250,000. The chairman, Earl Kil-
morey, is coming out to see to things. This co. is also
working some leases in the Stuart Town camp, and has
already cause to regret it. The Aladdin’s Lamp, an off-
shoot, paid £40,000 in divs. during the year, making
£140,000 for four years. When the Wentworth mine was
floated some six years ago, there was absolutely no
payable ore in sight, though the prospectus and ex-
perts did not exactly say so. Tens of thousands were
spent before any was found, but when discovered,
the ore chutes were rich enough for anything, including
white-washing.

Prospectus of the Mount Ada Gold-Mining Co.,
Kanowna (W.A.), is advertised in The Bulletin. The
property covers 36 acres, situated within 2J4 miles of the
Kanowna railway that is to be, and 14 miles from the pre-
sent terminus at Kalgoorlie. Four reports on the exten-
sive lode formation, and bulk samples assayed therefrom
hold out gorgeous hopes to the investor —in fact, Mr. F. G.
de Visme Gipps, M.E., states, in a confidential whisper, as
it were, that he can strongly recommend the Mount Ada
to any personal friends who may be listening. The vendor
wants 60,000 shares and £3OOO cash. 50,000 are offered to
the public to be issued as f ully paid up upon payment of
3s. on appli. and 3s. on allot. Why they should be called
10s. shares on the prospectus when they only cost 6s.
is more than an ordinary mortal can comprehend, but
the enigma is of no consequence.

* * * *
Great Boulder Main Reef (W.A.) : 259 tons,
2280 z. “ The poorness of the yield is due to gold being
free only in part, while the tellurides were not saved.”
Any excuse for a poor crushing. And why were the tel-
lurides not saved ? The fact is that the Boulder lode is
much poorer before it reaches the Main Reef boundary, a
circumstance known to all who have examined the parent
mine, and the telluride ores are neither as rich nor as
plentiful as the public have been led to think.

• * * *
Exemption from labor-covenants has always
been the burning question of Westralia, more so than in
all the other fields of Australia. Why ? No mine can pos-
sibly become rich under exemption. The Govt., of course,
is fencing the question. The Mines-Minister, to an oppos-
ing deputation It was the policy of the Govt, to do
everything possible to encourage development, but the
interests of capital must also be considered. He held a
letter from a large English co. threatening withdrawal
from the country if exemption were not granted, and,
under such circumstances, it would not be wise to force
them. How many similar letters is he likely to get?
Where the grounds urged were genuine, or not sufficient,
exemption would not be granted. Sheer humbug. If this
policy were enforced, there would be more leases forfeited
in W.A. this year than new ones taken up.

* * * *
E.F. (Adelaide): North Long Tunnel has every
probability of development into a cheerful co., but the
writer does not expect it to get there for a while.
Co. fooling around at too shallow a level (1900 ft.),
instead of deepening 200 or 300 ft. more, where the prospect
of cutting the northern run of Long Tunnel stone would
seem an absolute certainty. Yet hope, though, that the
6ft. stone recently cut in the 1900 ft. north level may
develope well, but if that does not happen the co. will
proceed to sink 200 ft. —which means five months’ work—
and then to drive 500 ft. or 600 ft. for the reef. None of the
L.T. reef drives are within 500 ft. of the N.L.T. boundary,
so it is impossible to be explicit about the small bit of
thin reef the latter has in its south level.
• * • •
The report of the Bendigo District Gold Mines,
an English co., shows that of a total expenditure of
£4574, more than half went in directors’ fees and other
management expenses ! It isanon-div. payer. What the
exs. would be if it paid regular divs., Heaven only knows.
Probably the directors and legal managers of all the div.-
paying mines of Bendigo do not draw this amount yearly,
it takes “a terrible good mine” to stand Bull’s system of
management.

The Australian Trading World (London):
“ We hear that three claims of 30 acres each immediately
adjoining the Victorian Mt. Morgan Co’s property have
been sent to London for flotation. This extraordinary
gold discovery is described as likely to eclipse even the
Mt. Morgan mine, and will serve to at once revive the
mining industry of Victoria.” Much of this sort of hog-
wash appears in the same rag in relation to other mines.
It is read by mining men principally for amusement.

was probably never so dull as at present. Over-specula-
tion has gruelled some of the big men ; Westralia has taken
away many of the small men with their little capital, and
the Towers is just now engaged rolling up its sleeves and
spitting on its hands preparatory to making a big jump
into New Guinea.

The Australian Natives’ Association does
not flourish much outside Melbourne. A venomous
correspondent sends to The Bulletin from an
Australian capital an account of the Anniversary
Day dinner of the local branch :
It was sparsely attended, and the President arrived late,
hot and out of breath, with sundry bulky newspaper par-
cels under his arm, which, when they came in contact
with each other, emitted a melodious clink, pleasant to
the ears of the expectant A.N.A. When unwrapped these
parcels were seen by the breathless diners to contain the
“liquor” for the evening—a donation from the Presi-
dent, as he took good care to announce. The “liquor”
consisted chiefly of lager beer and one bottle of that
variety of gin known as “ squareface,” and it was hailed
with delight by the A.N.A. The President was disgusted
to find that they had been unable to restrain their
appetites pending his arrival, and vented his spleen upon
them by attacking the gin with some ferocity. Then the
beer was passed round, and the President could not be
prevailed upon to part with the “squareface” until the
beer was finished ; then, after having poured out a bum-
per for himself, in apparent fear that he would never see
it again, he handed it over to the tender mercies of the
others. While the gin was going round
But this is rank blasphemy. The Bulletin is
full of patriotism, even if it isn’t full of gin, and
will stop the venomous correspondent right here.
On second thoughts, The Bulletin is very “ full ”
of Patriotism.
* * * *
Melb. : “Last night I dropped into the
Church of England Chinese mission church, Little
Bourke-street —Lilly Buck. There was a babble,
emanating chiefly from young ladies, each teach-
ing a Chinaman to read the Bible. I counted 17
nice damsels, and there were more, altogether
over 20, each with a sleek Chow beside her. Other
Celestials had to be content with older ladies, and
some with men. Imagine the disgust of those
who come in too late for welly ni’. And welly ni’
it is, with heads chumming together, and glances
from the intelligent, languishing, almond eyes
into those of Missy Smith, Missy Blown, Missy
Jones, or Missy Lobinson.”
Photo., The Falk Studios.
Miss ROSE BLANEY, Maoriland’s Prima-Donna.

at any time.

One of the British army generals
has threated to forbid his—haw !-
bands to play in London if the—haw !
masses don’t bare their heads before, the

* * * *
Ovariotomy is now performed
(writes “ A Travelling Australian ”) in
the London hospitals for cancer of the
breast. Watson-Cheyne, the celebra-
ted surgeon-specialist on cancer, has
found that the operation causes the
breasts to wither up, and deprives the
cancer of the necessary nourishing pas-
ture. Should this treatment prove a
success, a formidable female curse will
be knocked off its perch.
* * * *
The clerical person creates his own
atmosphere. The individual shaves off
his moustache and fences his neck with
a white board at his peril, for the build-
ing he inhabits is inoculated forthwith.
The office of a Sydney daily is infected
with the parson microbe. The visit-
ing stranger instinctively doffs his hat
as he enters the sacred portal, and, on
the approval of a black-coated clerk,
automatically rummages his pockets for
a stray threepenny-bit. The passages
are like the aisles of a church, every
office seeming a pew, and one leaves
uneasily, as though the eyes of a
shocked congregation were on him be-
cause he had not waited for the Bene-
diction.
A lady was stuck-up the other night m
Paddington, Sydney, by a young man in a sulky
and robbed of her purse, containing a few shillings.
The stuck-up girl and the four-bob robbo !

A donor of fish lately wrote to the secre-
tary of a Northern hospital asking why he hadn’t
acknowledged his gift through the press? The
sec. replied that he thought everyone had heard
about it. Anyhow, the fish smelt loudly enough
to inform the whole district that it had arrived at
its destination.
* * * *
Rude query : “I have never
known a strict vegetarian appear as
co-respondent in this court,’ quoth
an Australian divorce judge. Very
likely—for physiological reasons.
The vegetarian who does pursue
such enterprise is on the face of it
not a strict vegetarian. And, any-
how, was there ever a strict vege-
tarian, not excluding even that
‘Biblic herbivore,’ as the late
Baron von Mueller would have
called him, Nebuchadnezzar?”
* * *
At a certain Australian sea-
side resort the conspicuous figure
to-day is a long slab of a gospel-
thumper. From sunrise till break-
fast, every morning, he roosts upon
a seat on the ocean beach with an
umbrella opened over his head,
and a Bible opened upon his lap.
And from that post, with the aid
of a pair of 40-horse-power field-
glasses, he squints through a hole
in the umbrella at the women who
come down to wash themselves in
the sand and surf. He has a worn
and haggard appearance has this
gospel-thumper, due doubtless to
looking on the woman when she is
wet for many, many years.
* * *
Melb. suburban courts seem
to have fixed a scale of alimony
“to suit the times.” The amount
allowed wives who are living apart
from their husbands is 7s. 6d. a
week—2s. 6d. more than is ordered
to be paid for the support of an
illegitimate baby. Meanwhile the
lady-orators of the W.C.T.IJ. are
stumping the place, raging about
Women’s Rights.

Acting-Gov. Madden, presiding over the
A.N.A. bike carnival at Melb. Exhibition, was a
pathetic sight. He stood awhile in the centre of
the arena, with redhot sunshine streaming down
upon his belltopper, a person of no importance
beside the unlovely candidates who mustered on
the track. His position was so utterly destitute
of pomp and circumstance that he presently
flopped down upon one of the few chairs present—
a hard, wooden-bottomed, armless chair, manufac-
tured for kitchen purposes. Madden’s coat-tails
hung casually over the sides of this vulgar piece of
furniture as lie sat there, trying to make the best
of an undignified job. Once or twice he seemed to
be considering whether it were possible to re-
arrange himself effectively, but, the case being
palpably hopeless, he gave up the idea, and lapsed
into pensiveness. He was, perhaps, forming a

* * * *
An amateur entertainment at Frankston
(Melb.) the other Saturday amounted to very
little more than a Madden show, inasmuch as 11
artists on the programme included nine relatives,
by blood or marriage, of the dapper Acting-Gov.
Half-a-dozen Misses Madden took the stage
upon this amazing family occasion, with the cus-
tomary family assurance. Three of them gave a
capital terpsichorean display of those limbs known
as legs in unrefined circles, so now the world
is in a position to endorse the famous ruling by
papa Madden that a girl’s greatest glory is plump-
ness.
The medical superintendent
of a W.A. mining township hos-
pital called out recently to his
favorite black gin, and informed
her, partly by signs and partly by
words, that she must go to the
laundress and bring home his
washing. Now the doctor is so
erratic in his conversation, and
‘smiles so seraphically, that the
sole impression left upon the
maiden’s mind was his gorgeous
generosity. She returned not, and
the doctor, after waiting an hour,
opened his door to cooey. Judge
of his astonishment and fury when
he beheld Jemima attired in a
clean white singlet—and nothing
else; Judy and Carrie in white
shirts—and nothing else ; Lizzie
in pyjama jacket—and nothing
else ; Billy in pyjama pants ; and
others wearing a gleeful expres-
sion—and nothing else. These
simple children of the mulga un-
derstood the meaning of a Christ-
mas present better than the doc-
tor, whose tendency to profanity
was tempered by his belief in ata-
vism. “ I seemed,” he afterwards
explained, “ to hear a voice echo-
ing down the corridor of the past
saying, ‘As he is, you were ; as he
is you may be again. Behold thy
brother and sisters. ’ ” This is true
philosophy, but it cost about £1
10s.

Miss Rose Blaney, the recognised operatic
prima-donna of her native Maoriland, is among
the very few singers who have achieved a high
degree of culture within this hemisphere. During
the past seven years or so, Miss Blaney (who
nrst began to cultivate her taste for music at the
Dominican Convent, Dunedin) paid three educa-
tional visits to Melbourne, where a broken series
of studies under Madame Lucy Chambers, Madame
oimonsen, and James Brierley produced excellent
results. A pretty little lady, possessing what
bvengali termed the “ artist nature ” and a fair-
sized soprano voice of fine quality, Miss Rose
Blaney is to-day a really first-class singer. Dune-
din speaks in high terms of her acting ability.
Anyway, Miss Blarney’s Australian successes as a
concert-singer, chiefly in Melbourne and Ballarat,
are well known to this journal, and her portrait is
published herewith in token of admiring remem-
brance.

Crowned heads of Europe have always
fiven their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
’aris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “Violette Reine ” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Peeciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and aro
sure to be rushed by everybody.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free for 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*

* * * *
That orful tragedy at a Melbourne iron-
monger’s shop has added new terrors to death by
murder. In support of Phillips’ story re his vic-
tim’s wild career among women (the victim’s
salary, by the way, has been toned down to 70s.
per week) the ’Erald published a crazy-looking
letter from an alleged female describing her (?)
virtuous struggle with the deceased “tempter,”
and relating other incidents that either happened
in the writer’s diseased imagination, or were in-
vented with ulterior motives. At one point the
epistle states that “he (Brady) often sent poor
Willie Phillips with presents and letters urging
me to meet him (secretly). He tried by anger and
persuasion and neglect to see if he could gain his
ends, but, thank God, I was firm to a loving
mother’s teachings.” Herein is the cue for
poor Willie Phillips to add a postscript to
his confession, so the following extract from
the ’Erald deserves to be read carefully.
“Having succeeded in obtaining this strange
letter, a representative of the Herald was deputed
to see Mr. Pitcher, managing clerk for Messrs.
Fink, Best, and Hall. That gentleman, while ex-
pressing surprise* as to how it had come into our
possession, admitted that it had been received.
Asked whether Willie Phillips had been questioned
as to the woman referred to in the communica-
tion as having lectured Brady on the error of his
ways, Phillips replied that he could not recall any
particular woman who would answer the descrip-
tion, but would think over the matter.” Whilst
poor Willie is thinking over this matter The
Bulletin would like to suggest the propriety of
forming a league for the Protection of Dead Men’s
Characters from irresponsible slander. What a
pity it is that the late Brady can’t suddenly turn
up to identify his own corpse as that of his twin
brother, and reap swingeing damages from a fine
crop of libel actions !
Lady Clarke’s sister, Miss Snodgrass, in-
jured by a spring-cart whilst riding on the dummy
of a Melb. tram, cannot possibly recover. She is
paralysed from the hips downward.
Journalist : “ Melb. Herald did its record
circulation over the confession of Phillips. Surely
an editor or proprietor must be an enemy of
society, and not in the beneficent sense of Ibsen’s
man.”
*.* * *
X.: “The futility of supposing that the aver-
age policeman or detective will ever find anything
if left absolutely to himself has been once more
shown during the forage for the Butler victims.
Lee Weller’s body was found by two bush-
geographers, who put two and two together and
walked straight to the spot. Previous to which
the mighty foot of the ambulatory ‘copper’ had
been covering the adjacent country without result
for many days. The latest ‘ find ’at Parkes was
located by a small boy hunting for bees’ nests.
The thrown-up soil was staring everyone in the
face, and the boy had only to investigate it with a
twig and the murder was out. Nevertheless,
‘ The police of Forbes sub-district had thoroughly
searched the neighborhood, and must haVe passed
within 20 yards of the spot where the body lay
hidden.’ They were probably looking for a
‘due.’”
The police found Preston, the best-planted of all
Butler’s corpses to date, entirely “ on their own ; ”
but. as this fact spoils “X.’s” argument, it'is
omitted. Lee Weller was found by a local resi-
dent, who knew the country, and who had seen
Butler near the spot a day or two before the
murder. As the police had vague and conflicting
information to guide them, and necessarily couldn’t
have such a clear idea of Butler’s whereabouts as
the local resident past whose house he had
travelled, they probably did as well as “X.”
would have done in the circumstances. The
Forbes police had a district of say 50 square miles
to search; it is pitted all over with prospectors’
heaps ; and, in 50 square miles, they failed to
identify one particular 6ft. heap.
In searching for the grave of Lesagh, sup-
posedly a victim of the Glenbrook murderer, one
constable from the coast having got away by him-
self suddenly came on an old mallee hen’s nest and
was quite sure he had found the grave, a,t which
he began working away in great excitement.
There were ancient eggs in the nest, and, when he
probed it with a stick, it smelt accordingly, where-
upon he veiled exultantly. The crowd, attracted
by his noise, then came along and “tableau !

* * * *
Education-Minister Cockburn has greatly
offended the male teachers of his department by
his decision to appoint a lady school-inspector.
They see in this new departure a desire to cut
salaries. One irate headmaster, at a meeting held
to consider the subject, spoke with indignation of
the institution of “Govt, by the Petticoat, which
is at the bottom of all mischief.”
* * * *
To rouse popular indignation, Port
Adelaide licensed victuallers determined to
strictly observe the Sunday closing law during
January, but, unfortunately for the hotelkeepers,
the people submitted with good grace and no
grumbling was heard. On Jan. 22 an Adelaide
publican charged with Sunday selling was acquit-
ted on the ground of no evidence, and yet the per-
son who swore that he bought the drink was fined
for buying that very drink. Which recalls the
Crawford-Dilke case, wherein the jury found that

The Adelaide Hospital row seems at last
to be dead, as days have gone by without any
public reference being made to it. But hold !
Here it is again “ Nurse Wyllie has been dis-
missed from the Adelaide Hospital on the ground
that she divulged information regarding the
management of the institution. Nurse Wyllie
was before the hospital board of management
to-day, and was requested to give the name of the
person to whom she gave a certain letter for deli-
very to Hr. Cecil Corbin, but she stoutly refused
to divulge the name.” The Adelaide Hospital
row—it is immortal, and may yet change the des-
tinies of Australia.
* * * *
The brig Phyllis, which gave the Orient
men a chance for glory, and which now seems to
be in business as an ocean dead-beat, was for
years a regular Adelaide-Brisbane flour-carrier
till the steamers stepped in. She has been on the
Australian coast since 1861, and was built two
years previously at New Brunswick.
* » * *
The barque Phyllis—Fill us.

So strong is the ‘ ‘ Masonic dodge ” becom-
ing down South that numbers of prominent
brethren have ceased to show either symbols or
signs of the craft —as the only alternative to bank-
ruptcy.
At Maldon(Vic.),
recently , an unmarried
lady suicided by gun-
shot while the rest of
the family were away
at a picnic. Whether
she did it through
melancholy at being
left out of the bun-
struggle on the damp
grass, where the worm
always gets into the
salad, isn’t known.
The case is remark-
able chiefly because it
is very rarely indeed
that a woman guns
herself to death;
drowning and poison
are the regular me-
thods by which women
make their exit from
this world, but drown-
ing in well-watered
regions is a long way
ahead. In arid Aus-
tralia, however, the
suicidal lady often
finds that there isn’t
sufficient water avail-
able, unless she gets
rid of her soul in
some b o d y’s water-
tank, which leads to
ill-feeling, and is in-
jurious to the drink-
ing supply —to say
nothing of the fact
that the hole at the
top of a tank is not
large enough to let a
bustle through con-
veniently. Also, Aus-
tralia is a land of vast
distances, where the
chemist’s shop is often
a long way off; con-
sequently the Austra-
lian woman shoots her-
self oftener than most
women. And, judging
by the records, this is
almost the only country where the average woman
is a good enough shot to hit her own head once in
seven tries. In other countries, she mostly breaks
all the pictures and cripples the cow, and comes
out of the fray herself undamaged.

A short time ago, Balmain
was athrob with the vexations of a
conflict between members of friendly
societies and ministers of churches
over the Hospital Sunday-parada
question. The Sunday afternoon
display in the name of charity was
denounced by the clerics, who, find-
ing that the society-men were deter-
mined to hold to the custom of years,
inaugurated an opposition show on
their own account on a Saturday.
They were determined that the
sanctity of the Sabbath and the at-
tendance at the Sunday-schools
should not be tampered with. Thus
the hospital benefited from two
sources instead of one. But the
consistency of the antagonists to
Sabbath desecration has been ab-
ruptly upset by the publication of a
small hand-bill, announcing that
one of the clerios aforesaid would,
on a given Sunday, deliver a special
address to bicyclists at the close of
a cycle parade—proceeds in aid of
the hospital! This is investing the
bicycle with spiritual virtues hither-
to unthought of. Friendly-society
men might placate the clergy next
year by holding a cycling procession
in place of the customary march-past
with flag and drum.
An enterprising impresario lately ob-
tained a consignment of Japanese ladies and
opened a palin-reading business in South Brisbane
(Q-). The local Mayor got to hear of the affair,
and, mustering a few bye-laws, hied him to the
scene. He found the tone of the house to be
blandly genteel; but as the only English which
the Nipponites could manage was—“ All li; ten
silling ; come agen,” a dark suspicion disturbed
the Mayor’s soul, and the strangers were emptied
out of the locality.

A man was arrested on a just-arrived M.L
steamer at Sydney last week for having thrown
vitriol in the face of his successful rival in the
affections of a barmaid, whom he, the acid-
thrower, had followed from Sydney to Maoriland.
Last week, too, at Perth, W.A., one Baker first
tried to shoot Miss Silvester, a barmaid, and then
hacked her throat badly. Barmaids are deadly :
it is probably the combination of wine and women
that makes the trouble.
Apropos: Sydney’s handsomest barmaid, a
dark Tasmanian, has just been exported to Wes-
tralia.
* * » *
Counter attractions—barmaids and free
lunch.

The story of the £5OOO gold-robbery from
the Oceana at Melbourne is another commentary
on the already well-understood fact that if you
hang a bag of gold on a large shrub in
a public place it will quite probably go a-rnis-
sing. The gold - room of the Oceana was
approached by two hatchways, and the chief
officer kept the keys—hanging on a nail in his
cabin. The keys of the gold-room itself were
in the care of the captain, but whether he
habitually left them on the bowsprit or whether
they were kept in the coal-bunker doesn’t appear.
The locks on the gold-room were apparently
nothing in particular, for they were burst open
with a small crowbar “ without much force.” The
key of one hatch went a-missing off its nail, but
though there was £209,000 on board, and the
missing key gave comparatively easy access to
it, no one seems to have worried in the least.
After this it would appear that the only really safe
way to convey bullion is to carry it in the lee
scuppers ; the gold-room with its lock which can
be burst open with a crowbar, and the hatch with
one lost key and the other key hanging con-
veniently on a nail, are a howling mockery.
SERENADE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, the township’s swathed in slumber
With moonlight as coverlet,
And the night-winds weave rare dreams that
Fold the sleepers in their net.
On such nights of old came lovers
Singing their faint serenades,
And the sweetest music idled
To the ears of listening maids.
And I would, amid these drooping
Trees- that tremble, sigh and start,
Tune a mandoline to whisper
The song throbbing in my heart,
So that in your heart an echo,
Love of mine, might swoon to-night
Of this exquisite pain of loving
And this torture of delight.
January, ’97.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.

A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, Feb. 2, 1897.
My Dear Myee—
The Victoria Club, in a respectable, hesitating
way, is “ taking up” theatrical stars. On Friday
last, Mrs. and Mr. Brough (the “ Mr.” is only an
appendage in a woman’s club) were entertained at
an afternoon swarry; also, Miss Austin, Miss
Temple and Mr. Titheradge. Women’s idea of
entertaining is filling small rooms insufficiently
ventilated with an extra-large-sized crowd, shut-
ting doors and windows, and generally playing
Black-hole of Calcutta. To keep up the tempera-
ture, hot tea and coffee are turned on at intervals,
and the subsequent groans of the victims are
sternly repressed while music plays.
There was a large, admirably-arranged pro-
gramme of events which did not come off. The list
of non-starters included Mr. Sid. Long and Mr.
Lambert, who would have done a lightning-sketch
if there had been space to rear an easel up. As a
brains-and-beauty show the gathering was fairly
representative. The all-admired Mrs. Brough was
youthful in a black silk skirt and an irrelevant
bodice of rosy satin veiled with black. Some
women gave away their age recklessly by wearing
low collars and laying bare the year-tallying lines
which the neck shows relentlessly. The pro-
prietress was gay in grass-lawn, and the hon. sec.
wore bronze silk with daisies.
The last but one of the “pleasant Saturday
afternoons ” arranged by the Society of Artists in
the Temperance Hall, Pitt-street, was held on
Saturday. A satisfactory section of the public
drifted in and was entertained with tea and con-
versation, music and sketching. Next Saturday
the drawing for the Art Union will take place, and
agitated subscribers will then know for certain
who has won the peacock-blue corrugated iron
roof in the yellow landscape, and the overworked
lavender pair of boots, and Pyrmont Bridge on
Judgment Day, and all the other pictures.
A solemn function occurred at Her Majesty’s
Theatre on Thursday evening, when the prizes
won at Anniversary Day regatta were presented
to winners by the Mayor in presence of a crammed
house adorned with a solitary banner inscribed
with a gigantic “ Q ” thrown over the d.c. railings
like a life-buoy. There was a painful pause in the
course of Act 3, and a long train of notables filed
sorrowfully on the stage and gazed with fixed
smile on the footlights, while his Worship ladled
out compliments all round in a tragic way as if he
were understudying the Silver King’s soliloquy, but
forgot his lines when he spoke about the successful
regattas and had to be prompted by the sec. from
behind. The Commodore of the P.A.Y.C. showed
much white vest and expansive smile, and slapped
his neighbor’s back; but every one felt it was
hollow mirth, and when the procesh gave three
stifled cheers and all shuffled nervously off again
the audience gave a sigh of relief and felt that
the worst was over.
Several performers at Miss Beatrice Griffiths’
concert at the Town Hall on Anniversary night
failed to turn up, but the blanks in the programme
were filled in with encores. The three sisters
Griffiths made very blushful and modest appear-
ances, and the eldest is one of those infrequent
girls who distributes for publication a photo, of
herself which doesn’t do her justice. Her tiny
hands thundering on the key-board are as surpris-
ing as a butterfly working a hand-organ. The
audience consisted mostly of girls, all wearing hats
looking as if they came out of the basket labelled
“ marked down, seized by the bank” in front of
an insolvent draper’s. Next day the same sort of
demonstrative farewell was given at Circular Quay
to the pianiste, who departed for Europe to
have the rest of her musical education made in
Germany.
Two cycling celebrities lately in Sydney nearly
had their life-friendship wrecked owing to the
same old cause. Only the cause isn’t very old in
this case, and has large, round, black-edged blue
eyes, reddened cheeks, and tow-colored, touzled
locks, and such a voice as the public imagines the
Swanhilda’s captain uses when expostulating with
his crew. And the two went every night to hear
her sing and see her dance, and tried to be first in
with bribes of oysters and champagne, until No.
1, who felt he was making all the running, grieved
to see that No. 2 was still imagining himself “ in
it,” and told him kindly, but firmly, that the
charmer was going to meet him (No. 1) that very
night when the theatre was out. Then No. 2
produced a scrawly note making an appointment
with him for the same time. So they agreed to
wait for the false one and have it out. But a cab
was waiting at the stage-door containing a power-
ful-looking No. 3, with whom the damsel drove off
without glancing at the two men who were left
lurking in the shade. Then they shook hands
feelingly, had a drink together, and lived happily
ever after.
In “ Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” at the Royal, there is
one opportunity of dress display in the ancestral
halls of Chepstow, where the naughty Lady
Kesteven, with the unspellable front name, is
ousted from her possessions by the rightful heiress,
in a chest-revealing black satin. Aristocracy on
the stage ascends as the frock edge descends ; the
higher the title the lower the gown. A duchess
on the boards usually badly wants a chest-pro-
tector. The guests standing around, in the scene
where Blanche is hurleCk from society, take it all
out in stage jewels and a haughty sweep of their
trains at the supposed guilty girl, and express
their smothered emotions by opening and shutting
their fans excitedly.
One of the two fortunate legatees of a recently-
deceased wealthy widow used to be a folder in the
office of a defunct Melb. newspaper. He was met
in the street a few days ago, and congratulated on
his luck by a former reporter on the same paper.
The legatee thanked the poor pressman, and, after
some condescending remarks, said, “ I must leave
you, dear boy, here is my carriage,” and a mag-
nificent vehicle and pair drove up and bore off the
well-gilt inheritor, who now shares with his sister
a newly-attained family-crest and an annual
.£40,000. The latest matrimonial prize has an
honorary billet as inspector under the Fisheries
Act, and was instrumental in getting 3d. a head
placed on cormorants, the fishes’ enemy. He is a
teetotaller, and owns a paying brewery.
Bathers in the Bondi baths one ladies’day lately
observed an altercation going on in the dim dis-
tance between the caretaker and an apparently
obtrusive stranger who wished to enter. They
would have been much fluttered had they known
that the intruder was an enterprising, up-to-date
Chinaman armed with a kodak who wanted to
come in and snap-shot the bathers. He made bis
request with much guilelessness, and seemed be-
wildered and hurt when repelled with great re-
pulsion.
The latest exports from Sydney are two beau-
teous creatures from a city hotel shipped to Wes-
tralia with fabulous stipends to increase the
attractions of Wallace Brownlow’s Kalgoorlie
palace. King Hongree is doing things in a mag-
nificent way over there ; his waiters look like first-
class stage menials, and in his new r6le he is as
popular as ever, and less harassed by sentimental
notes from adoring females, which was one of the
reasons alleged for his leaving the boards.
An amateur tenor on a moonlight harbor ex-
cursion was pouring forth his soul in “Maud,”
and passionately warbled “ Shine out little head,
running over with curls.” Nobody laughed,
because nobody ever does listen to a song; but
“ running ” instead of “ sunning ” is about as bad
a break just there as could be made.
In a fashionable Sydney feeding-place there is
a large glass skylight in the roof. Just under it
one afternoon an elegantly-tailored johnny was
sitting, drinking tea with a girl. There was a
low growling and scuffling on the roof, which the
two absorbed tea-drinkers took no notice of, when
suddenly, with a loud yell, a cat fell through the
open window and alighted abruptly on the man’s
neck, clawing and scratching at his back hair.
He was too much astonished to do anything but
jerk up his cup and deluge his face with scalding
tea, and then his impression was that somebody
had felled him from behind, and that gore was
streaming all over his face. It took fully fifteen
minutes to convince him that he wasn’t dead.
The record short engagement was gone through
by a Ballarat (Vic.) girl last week. She and her
lover “ had words ” as they wandered by the lake
shore. A passing stranger wandered vaguely into
the quarrel, advised the girl to “ chuck her bloke ”
and take on the new-comer as an escort home.
With touching confidence this advice was at once
acted upon, and the stranger was invited in, and
proceeded to make himself solid with Ma and the
rest of the family. Next day, with the aid of a
parson, six bottles of whisky and two friends, the
fascinating stranger married the trusting maid,
and one day later he bolted, taking with him the
bride’s marriage-certificate in a tin-box. A daily,
chronicling this headlong matrimony, guardedly
states that the girl “ gives ” her age as 25, and is
said “to be good-looking.” After reading thus
far the world will have concluded that the
girl was deceived by a designing villain. But no
—the bridegroom, said to be of excellent character,
has turned up again as good as new, his absence
having been merely due to the quality of the
whisky, and the pair are going to live happily for
ever after.
A first-night audience at Her Majesty’s on Satur-
day beheld the “ Silver King ” apparently played
on stilts. The cast is a typical cornstalk one ; and
all tower up close to and over the six-feet level.
Miss Eitzmaurice Gill, as Olive Skinner, creates
surprise by wandering round her villa at night in a
to-be-continued-in-our-next white drapery with
tree-foliage gummed on ; her purply evening-gown
is also florally ornate and pretty. Harrie Ireland
wore some cunningly-arranged fur on Nellie Den-
ver’s first-act gown, and little Daisy Ireland
shows that she shares the family inheritance of
sunsetty hair.
The water in the new swimming-baths at Cool-
gardie is mineral, and leaves “ a faint pink
deposit.” This must have an alluring effect on
bathers, making them look like freshly-powdered
babies. A thorough soaking in such water ought
to be recommended as a complexion-restorer, and
if it could only be patented in popular bathing-re-
sorts, enterprising property-holders would gladly
the multitudinous sea incarnadine—or words
to that effect—as an attraction to summer
swimmers.
Affable Emily Soldene is in Sydney once more,
and has given her opinion to interviewers that
“ Australians are far more go-ahead and advanced
in their way of thinking than the English.”
Emily has written a book which, she says, has
“ some rather advanced views in it.” If the book
were a treatise on mission work in China, the
authoress would probably have remarked on the
great and absorbing interest the people of this
continent feel in lightening the dark places of this
earth, or if she had written a poultry-keeper’s
manual, she would claim that the growing industry
here was chicken-farming.
The League of N.S.W. Wheelmen has been
agitating the subject of satin and wool as a racing
costume material. Wool, adopted for hygienic
reasons by the Yic. League, was voted out in
favor of the more gaudy satin “in cardinal colors
only.” When the suburban girl goes to “ barrack ”
for her favorite scorcher, she likes to see all the
part of him that isn’t out of his clothes sweltering
in shiny silk; wool is too dull and lustreless.
And the bare-legged hero clothes himself to
attract.
The loan collection of pictures which will
brighten the new additions to the Art Gallery in
April will give the public a glimpse of art treasures
carefully acquired at vast expense by local
millionaires on continental tours and hoarded in
the seclusion of newly-built, plaster-smelling
private galleries. The enthralled visitor is taken
round to visit best English-made landscapes of the
style where one man paints the trees and another
genius puts in the cows, or “ execute-while-you-
wait ” figure-scenes with the beginning-of-the-cen-
tury lovers in every possible attitude. Then there
are the “old masters.” But that is too sad a
subject with which to blight this letter. How
many Australian canvasses will be in the loan
exhibition ?
Theforthcomingcathedral fair —oneof theattrac-
tions of which is to be the inevitable tug-of-war—is
agitating the minds and the pockets of the faithful
in various suburbs. The Exhibition building was
taken one recent evening for a dance by a committee
of ladies whose names made the circular look like the
map of Ireland. At the Balmain Town Hall there,
was also a “ Myee ” dance, though the dancers
were mostly from the Emerald Isle. The “ Grand
Australian Fair ” will be held next Easter, and all
these preliminary outbreaks are to raise money to
furnish stalls.
Girls ambitious of securing an imported husband
sometimes have a deal of worry in regard o
exotic article. I heard of one damsel engaged to
a fair and faithless new-ebum who neglected to
turn up at the “betrothal dance arranged to
show him off, and now she doesn t exactly know
whether her engagement is on or off. A so,
another Sydney girl brought out her fiance along
with half Regent and Bond-streets in millinery
per mail steamer on her family s return Rom
foreign parts, and now the favored youth is
boarded and lodged at Pa’s expense and having a
good time generally while his adored one shows
him round to all her friends.
There was racing and chasing at the Lyceum
when the bailiffs arrived and wished to seize all
the wardrobes. One tall blonde actress was lucky
in getting all her gear away unharmed, but the
only course open to another was to wear away her
clothes on her back, a garment at a time, so. that
half-a-dozen visits to the theatre revealed her in
as many different characters, and her gaily-clad
appearance in the streets caused great surprise.
The pleased smirk of society wedding portraits
creates a craving in the breasts of some newly-
married folks to publish joyful pictures of them-
selves in all the illustrated papers, that a,n envious
public may gaze on them and yearn to distinguish
itself likewise. But some prefer to, hide their
rapturous expression from the world’s gaze. A
bridegroom of the other day who was thus pictured
along with his wife unbeknownst to either, waxed
more and more annoyed as he worked his way
through the weekly papers to see himself and her-
self smiling back at themselves in wholly un-
authorised photo, reproductions on the picture-
page. He is now consulting solicitors with a view
to unravelling the libel act and trying to bring it
in under the “ contempt or ridicule,” section.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
It is time to cast an eye of suspicion upon the
handicapping of bike-racers in Melbourne. Among 20
Australians who achieved places in their heats for the
A.N.A. Wheel Race, there were 10 who had taken part
in Austral finals one year or other, if not several years in
succession. W. J. C. Elliott, C. O. Tebbutt, T. O. Fisher,
and O. S. Prowse may be selected from the aforesaid
lucky 10 for handicap comparison. Tebbutt and Prowse
each won heats in the last Austral, and whereas Prowse
was put back 40yd. for his good performance on that
occasion, young Tebbutt was advanced 30yd.— i.e., he
met Prowse on 70yd. better terms in the A.N.A. event
than in the Austral. Also, for non-apparent reasons, the
veterans Elliott and Fisher got 20yd. longer start at Melb.
Exhibition than when they distinguished themselves
seven weeks previously on M.C.C. ground. The same
encouraging treatment was served out pretty evenly all
round, it is true, but, as a rule, the Austral starters
who didn’t run into places were not more favored this
time than the men who did, which explains why they
(the unplaced ones) got left again. And the occasional
exceptions are somewhat glaring. Prowse, as stated
above, had to go back 40yd. from his Austral mark, and
J. E. Clinton, who finished second to E. Perryman in an
Austral heat, was now put back 50yd., whilst E. P.
received the usual 20yd. extra, meeting his runner-up on
70yd. better terms. Clearly Tebbutt was absurdly
favored at the expense of such as Prowse and J. E.
Clinton.
W. O. Jackson’s case calls for special enquiry,
in The Bulletin’s opinion. Jackson won an Austral heat
with apparently a lot in hand, but his performance in the
final was not at all brilliant. Nevertheless, the handi-
capper for the A.N.A. race put Jackson back from 180yds.
to 70yds., and so he met Tebbutt at 140yds. disadvan-
tage as compared with their Austral starts. Jackson had
been recently disqualified for a term by the precious
L.V.W., but under protest the ban was removed in time
to enable the up-country candidate to start for the big
prize. Having earned a place in his heat, ill-fated
Jackson was disqualified once more at the instance of
G. Carpenter, who seems to be a recognised pet
of the Melb. bike authorities. This, then, is Jack-
son’s record as regards the A.N.A. Wheel Race. He
was the most unkindly handicapped competitor of the
whole crowd. He was disqualified from starting until
his few friends protested against the League’s action.
And when the League allowed him to compete, he was
knocked out of the final for “ erratic riding.” Query :
Would Tebbutt have been disqualified if he had happened
to bump somebody in his heat ?
The limit start in a two-mile wheel race should
be fixed at 300yds., even though this rule might necessi-
tate a placing of the “ cracks ” 20yds behind scratch.
Relfe, the wan Englishman, went slower than
M‘Donnell (40yds.) from the very start of his heat in the
A.N.A. race, and he had soon had enough of it. Porta
and Martin, as usual, were the struggling heroes of the
hour.
Two or three dangerous men were put out of the
A.N.A. Wheel Race by falls. Both Boidi and Grantor
ran like winners of their respective heats until the
asphalt smote them hip and thigh.
* * * *
Pither seems the most “ gone-off ” pedaller of
all the men who ranked among the first flight last year
whilst Porta, without doubt, is the most improved. But
the Italian’s appearance suggests that the time of his de-
generation draweth nigh. His face begins to show the
same peculiar pallor which marked Bilmartin’s iron phiz
when that persistent battler first comp.’ained of not feel-
ing well, and within two months, perhaps, Porta will need
to be assisted off his bike after putting in a great race
from scratch.
» • * *
The effort of running second for his heat in the
A.N.A. Handicap left Martin all agasp. A snap-shot
photo, of the over-wrought plugger, as he slowed down
when his task was done and the mob cried “ Good old
Bill ! ” would have been very well worth printing He
put back his head, opened his mouth, and gurgled
faintly, just as though Death had tapped him on the
shoulder.
The Bulletin herewith advises Martin to trv
the restorative effect of a trip back to ’Murka. He has
worked well and made a bit of money, and now the work
is telling on him—awfully.
Boidi is probably the coming bikist. Relfe
looks shockingly unhealthy, Lesna’s aspect is not pro-
nnsing, Bill Martin can’t spurt as he did, and our other
old friend. Porta, will not go on for ever. Boidi improves
steadily, ditto M'Donnell.
* * * »
Dear Bulletin, —Bike-race-reporting nuzzles
much. When bellow and Walne paced one another to
the fore in the Austral, over a grass track, the limit men
were blamed for not having gone faster. Now I read
that m the final heat of the A.N.A. race “the front
division made the pace pitilessly fast,” and yet the time
for the two miles (on asphalt) was a second slower than
the Austral record.—B.B.
* * • •
v T «t S, ! orting amateurishness of the League of
Vic. Wheelmen was shown in a ridiculous light at a recent
meeting of the League, when Sam. Winter proposed that
the Martin v. Lewis match should be “ hedged round
with conditions, so that if one man did not turn up the
other could not claim the race.” Observe how Winter’s
eate- money idea is calculated to ensure a genuine
struggle ! The man who seems very likely to get beaten
goes to the “ dead bird” and stipulates for a slice of the
prize money. Otherwise he will not turn up, and the
other fellow will get nothing.
* * * *
When one Snell set out to lower the Adelaide-
Melboume bicycle record he was solemnly warned by his
doctor that he was probably lopping 15 years off his life.
He was similarly warned in Feb., ’94. He is now 28years
old—has therefore already lost 30 years. If this record-
breaking business goes on much longer, Snell will, in pro-
cess of time, be a toothless veteran aged 515.
m * * *
Whv can’t a woman teach a woman to bike ?
J * * * *
Cycling has certainly the brutal characteristic
essential to a popular sport in a civilised, Christian com-
munity. On the Saturday previous to the A.N.A. event
in Melbourne, at the L.V.W. meeting, ambulance
stretchers were kept quite busy throughout the afternoon,
carting in the smashed-up riders, who came down in pairs
and bunches in every event. There were many dull,
sickening thuds on the Exhibition track on Foundation
Day ; and, the day after, the asphalt was found to be
richly illuminated with “ horrid swishes of blood.” The
Ballarat man, who was the first to come down, seemed to
have a good deal of his head and faoe ground off on the
raspy surface, and was, in addition, stripped of his riding-
pants, and left, unconscious and coverless, upon the
track under thousands of eyes, many of them feminine.
Five men got mixed up in a sanguinary hash in the
A.N.A. final, and two—Fontaine and Fisher—looked,
when gathered up, as if they had been run through a
steam reaper-and-binder. Wanted, a pneumatic india-
rubber suit for racing cyclists, on the principle of the
deep-sea diver’s dress.
* » * »
A Brisbane correspondent writes that the ram-
pageous biker rules the road in that city, the police being
too warm to protest. A “ scorcher ” considers himself
aggrieved when he knocks against any unmounted mortal,
and a law compelling pedestrians to carry lamps back and
front (like poultices) after nightfall would fill a long-felt
want.
* * • »
The track at Melb. Exhibition Grounds is far
from perfect, for all the improvements. About half the
80,000 who attended the A.N.A. sports amused themselves
staring into the back hair of the other half, or catching a
fleeting glimpse of color once in 15 minutes. Experts
declared the riding path O.K. when it was opened, but it
has four death-traps in the corner curves, where the men
are bowled over in almost every event. The management
allowed for this fault on Foundation Day by providing
surgeons and ambulance-waggons, but no coffins. A stack
of coffins in the centre of the ground would have shown
that the authorities meant it when they announced
“ every provision will be made for the convenience of
competitors.”

Young Tebbutt, who won the A.N.A. Wheel
Race, is a Rechabite and a Sunday-school teacher. Bil-
martin, by the way, is a teetotaller; also Porta. They
swill tea.

The females at bike-sports seem to have taken
up the study of anatomy. “ I can’t see the number,”
said one on A.N.A. day to her chum ; “ but I know it’s
him by his legs.”
* * * •
A week or two ago, when Bilmartin was accused
of cronk riding, he pleaded in indignant refutation that
he had backed himself for the event. It afterwards
turned out that by the League regulations birring by
competitors is forbidden, and that Bill had by his admis-
sion rendered himself liable to punishment. Beyond re-
marking the matter, however, the League of Yic. Weak-
men did nothing.
* • * *
The recent cricket-match, N.S.W. v. Yic., was
fairly fought out, and Yic. got beaten by close on 200 runs.
N.S.W. had the good luck to win the toss, but this was
more than balanced by Victoria’s double share of luck in
getting rid of so strong a batting side on a perfect wicket
for a miserable 159. Very likely the best English eleven
under similar conditions wouldn’t have got the same side
out for less than 275. Vic. therefore had every encourage-
ment at this stage, and still more when her team in the
first innings totalled 269 ; a lead of 110. This lead far more
than compensated for Vic. having to bat on the fourth
wicket should fine weather keep on, and the fine weather
did keep right along. The second innings of N.S.W.
brought out the full strength of the home team’s batting
in most consistent scores; Turner, with four, being the
only man not to get double figures. Donnan’s stubbornly
fine 84, without a chance, drove the first long nail into the
Vic. coffin, and after that Noble came with 153 not out.
Howell, the last man, came in to help Noble put on 110
for the last wicket; the innings ceased with the grand
score of 570. With 461 runs to face, Vic.’s chance was
dead and buried.

“ Jonah ” Jones is credited with the intention
to start cycle racing. He is now employed as a ground
man on Adelaide Oval at £3 a week ; the Sussex offer has
been finally declined. S.A. badly needs two more profes-
sional bowlers, as recent matches have proved. With the
decline of Giffen, S.A. depends entirely on lighting Jonah.

Cricketer M. A. Noble is brother to Harry
Noble, N.S.W. Deputy-Registrar-General, himself an
expert with the bat. Another brother is Arthur Noble,
the well-known Sydney baritone, and a fair exponent of
lawn tennis. M. A. Noble was a chorister at St. Mark’s,
Darling Point, and was equally efficient in vocal exercises.

fourth-rate junior (N. Brunswick) to a premier club at one
jump.

It would be a startling contract to under-
take to provide every man and woman with his or
her own dead body, but the Edison Electric Parlor
at 162 Pitt-street, Sydney, will go a long step
further and show everybody his or her skeleton on
a moment’s notice for the reasonable sum of 6d.
The X rays have been utilised by Edison for show
purposes, and hence the public gets an opportunity
to step into its grave long before its corpse has de-
cayed and to take a full and steady view of its
bones without any of the troublesome turning up
of soil necessary in inspecting a skeleton hitherto.
The X rays should facilitate cremation, because
when the public fully realises what a rattlesome
ramshackle gimcrack its own skeleton is, it will
want to go down to its tomb in the size and form
of a packet of salts.*
J H. SQUIRE & CO.,

Personal Items.
Editor Haddon, of Melb. Argus, enters
his 59th year on Feb. 8.
The defence to be set up for the lad Phil-
lips will, it is said, be maniacal frenzy.
George Turner (Vic.) is the only Aus-
tralian Premier who began public life as an aider-
man.
Pastor Bevan is going to England but (like
all the rest) he means to return. “ Lead us not
into temptation.”
Butler’s fondness for jewellery is likely to
be gratified. A watch and a pair of bracelets are
being kept for him at S. Francisco.
Daniel Ferron, the discoverer of the rich
Fryerstown (Vic.) reef, died last week in—afflu-
ence ? No, in Oastlemaine Benevolent Asylum.
Mrs. Saunders, mother of the Misses
Saunders, Melb. girls, murdered while missionizing
in China, is, it is said, on the point of going as a
missionary to the same region.
Dr. Thomson (of Brisbane, Q.): “If I
had to choose between sharing the cabin of a
steamer with a consumptive or a leper, I would
unhesitatingly choose the leper.”
Dr. Donovan, Q.C., who left Sydney the
other day with, as many believed, the intention
of entering a monastic order, has booked himself
to return to Australia in two years.
The slaughtered Mrs. Silas Harding, mis-
tress of many thousand pounds, was the lady
whom it was said ex-Premier Patterson was to
wed, but Death broke off the match.
The new Dean of Ballarat—selected by
the Bishop during his recent visit to Hingland—is
a lively young man, who was only ordained in
1891. He ought to come well off for slippers.
N.S. Wales is afflicted with 11 knights, one
of whom—old Saul Samuel—is an absentee, and
two baronets—Nicholson and Cooper—both of
whom sport their glory and lucre in England.
Baroness Lisgar, whose will is now before
Sydney courts for official seal, recalls the urbane
Governor Young, who reigned in N.S.W. when
one Charley Cowper was Premier and another
Charley Cowper clerk of the Executive Council.
Benjamin Hoare, the Age leader-writer,
has developed into a fine lecturer, with a grand
air. The Argus is unfortunate in this regard. It
started Howard Willoughby off lecturing last
year, but Willoughby stutters and has a mincing
manner and a cracked-pot voice. A year or two
ago the Argus had a reporter named M‘Gregor—
now in England—who would insist on lecturing in
favor of Socialism.

Sachse, Vic. M.L.C., had the thread of his
discourse badly tangled while orating at the
A.N.A. banquet. “This association,” he ob-
served, “ should direct itself to the collection of
Australian antiquities.” He paused to let the
scintillating idea soak into the assemblage when a
ribald youth at the end of the table blandly en-
quired, “ What’s wrong with the Upper House? ”
The remainder of Saehse’s speech was unheard.
