to-day. The little man persists in balancing him-
self against the vinous champions on the Licensing
Bench every Thursday. As usual
with the pertinacious, he is given
his own way. But it never pays to
lay to the public book. Lormer
has scuttled out of his cosy book-
shop in Bourke-st., and shrunk
to half the size in Little Collins-
street. Even the ‘ unco guid ’
refused to subscribe to his
paltry testimonial when they
heard he had addressed the
May Sunday meeting on
Yarra bank. The capitalist
is pious.”
“Oh, pa,” said a
little girl who had been
taken to the races for
the first time, “ why
have those men ” (point-
ing to the bookmakers)
“all got broken
noses ? ” “ Probably
because they de-
served them,’ re-
plied Pa, senten-
tiously. And how
did Mr. Butler-
Harwood, of Glen-
brook, get his
broken nose ? Did
it happen as some
intended victim
suddenly turned
round in the grave that was to be, and let out
with a straight left when he caught his “ mate ”
with his finger on the pistol-trigger ? However
Mr. Harwood came by that nose, you may depend
he deserved it.
M‘Gl. : “That Australasian portrait is
nothing like young Phillips, the Melb. murderer.
Instead of being a good-looking, plump, healthy
youth of apparently 15, the real Phillips is an
anaemic, under-sized, thin, hollow-chested, typical
cheap clerk, who might pass for 25. Who told
you he was Jewish ? There’s certainly no Hebrew
blood in him and nothing Hebrew in his appear-
ance, while, as far as religion is concerned, he was
a member of Dr. Bevan’s choir and taught a class
in the attached Sunday-school.
Joseph Chamberlain owns
station and other properties in
Maoriland, Australia, Bermuda,
and other far-apart countries. This
inspires his zollverein anxieties.
A very sad Australian young
person called Jerome Dyer, writes
a London correspondent, rose at a
recent meeting of the Colonial In-
stitute and delivered himself of a
harangue on “ Victoria and its
Resources.” Jerome’s facts were
as old as the convict hulk Success,
and his figures, when he had any,
were out of date and sadly erratic ;
also, he was pompous and weari-
some and despondent. If Victoria
has many resources like Dyer the
fact had better be concealed as
much as possible.
The Rev. J. Jones, who re-
tires from duty at Hunter’s Hill,
enjoys the unique distinction of
having on one occasion almost pre-
cipitated a war between England
and France. The French had
hunted Jones out of the Loyalty
Islands, where he had long been
gospeling, and the doughty parson
travelled to England for revenge.
He danced attendance assiduously
on Salisbury and worked up much
sultry indignation, but in the end
Salisbury declined to fight.
When Bryan O’Loghlen was
a burlesque Premier in Victoria,
his compatriots labored under a
grave injustice. At their St.
Patrick’s Day procession they
wouldn’t get their banner (a huge,
unwieldy structure on wheels)
under the telegraph-lines without
damaging the wires, or lowering
their banner and their dignity
also. The sympathising baronet
accordingly had all the wires in
t e city raised about 3ft., and
thereafter the gorgeous banner got
through without difficulty. “Thim
was great toimes for Oireland, en-
toirely. ”
Any rich and moderately vulgar Aus-
tralian who wants a title and is willing to pay for
it has now a golden opportunity. There is a
famine in India, and cash is being whipped up.
For a £50,000 donation a baronetcy could easily
be arranged.

Q £ f our m ii eg) every fine day. His son Walter—

healthful thing.

A sea-captain, who had been in Sydney
port ten years ago, called at Colonial-Secretary
Brlinker’s office last week with a packet of curios
picked up in his voyage, which he intended to
present to Parkes I. The old shellback retired
with a tear in his eye.
JOB'S COMFORTER.
SPLODGE: “Yes, I seem to paint worse and worse
every day, until sometimes I feel like chucking
it altogether.”
SMUDGE: “ Oh, I wouldn't do that just yet awhile;

Words of wisdom from Melb. Coroner
Youl (at the Brady inquest): “Itis a mistake for
a man to write to a woman, but there is no law to
prevent it. If young men will take my advice
they will never do it.” Epistolary silence is
golden.
Creffield, king of Sweer’s Island, has abdi-
cated, and sold his little territory for ,£l5O. For
30 years Creffield has reigned, the absolute
monarch of Sweer’s Island, and now he retires
voluntarily into public life. Australia has so
few royalties that the movements of any of them
are matters of absorbing interest.
Plain George Turner wore a very seedy
boxer hat, but could not live up to it. He has
become a K.C. M.G., and wears a belltopper now.
Once the man dominated the hat ; now, alas ! the
hat will dominate the man. The Bulletin is
anxiously looking for the Australian statesman
who possesses the courage of his battered hat.
J. G. Drake, M.P., has had his engage-
ment to a Brisbane (Q.) girl mentioned in the local
press. Drake was supposed to be a confirmed
celibate, and now he’s going to tackle a father-in-
law whose front name is Abraham —
What mystery to take !
A duck becomes a Drake !
Apropos Cardinal Moran, his alleged
Federal Convention candidature and clerical
opposition thereto, a very old Sydneyite laments
the “ good old days ” when parson Cowper, Father
Therry and Dr. Lang used to walk arm-in-arm
down George-street. The ancient does not remem-
ber if they were returning from the usual weekly
“ hangings.”
A correspondent writes to the Age in a
shocked hand complaining of the execrable taste
of Bourke-street shopkeepers who exhibit in their
windows portraits of the accused young man Phil-
lips. David Syme prints the letter, also portraits
of “the accused young man Phillips.” The
shocking taste of those shopkeepers lay in their
getting there first, presumably.
A member of a certain N.S.W. licensing
bench was re-appointed the other day, and, on
being congratulated in court, attempted a little
speech. He waved his fat palm towards the
magistrates, and said : “I am happy to be once
more aiding my collegians ” —meaning colleagues—
“and I promise to work as conscientiously in the
past as I have done in the future. ”
Young Sydney lawyer brings to a leader
of the bar his first brief, with which he has taken
immense trouble, and is thunderstricken when the
Q.C. hands it back to him with the remark—
“ You have left out the principal point in the
case.” The young man protested that he really
could not see what had been missed. “ There is
no cheque pinned to it,” dryly said the Q.C.
N.S.W. Justice Simpson on Bench ; bar-
rister Moriarty appears to examine bankrupt as to
alleged extravagant expenditure. Moriarty:
“You, sir, say you were very depressed finan-
cially, and yet you bought a brdugham ? ” Simp-
son, J., interrupting : “ Excuse me, Mr. Moriarty,
in the society in which I move we pronounce that
word ‘broom,’ thus saving one syllable.” Later
on, His Honor takes the floor with the same wit-
ness : “Please explain to the Court, sir, why you
bought a ‘broom.’ Would not an omnibus have
taken you to your business as well ? ” Moriarty,
loq.: “ Excuse me, your Honor—but in the society
in which / move we pronounce that word ‘bus,’
thus saving two syllables.”
The other Monday, when Bums’ statue,
in Sturt-street, was formally presented to Ballarat
city, one or two braw Scots seized the opportunity
to “ blow ”in an anti-Hibernian direction. They
remarked that every member of the committee
connected with the Burns effigy, a dozen years
ago, was still alive and hearty, whereas the lead-
ing instigators of the Tom Moore statue, erected
several years later, have gone to their silent
tombs. The explanation of which invidious
distinction may be that Scotch whisky is the less
hurtful, or that those whom the gods love die
younger than Scawtchmen.
One of Zox’s latest yarns implicates his
bosom friend, Speight. The two out driving.
Heat 100 in the shade. “Have a drink?” Stop-
page at wayside pub. Two big bottles of soda
with brandy ordered. Speight tenders a half-
crown, and receives Is. 6d. change. Zox expresses
an opinion that the drinks are well worth a shilling
each. Landlady takes back a shilling. Speight,
with Yorkshire blood beginning to rise, says
something about Zox being very liberal
with other people’s money. Interval. More
drinks, this time at Zox’s invitation. Five
navvies enter, and are immediately asked to have
drinks by the genial Speight. “ Thanks, wot’ll
yer ’ave, Bill ? ” Bill thinks he’ll ’ave a long beer,
“ Not at all,” says Speight, “take the same as us,
it’s a great drink.” So all seven are served, and
Zox, with Jewish blood rising, pays over 7s. In
buggy, Speight with great glee : “ Got you there,
old boy—that’s more than shent per shent.’’
Ed. Bulletin, —Lately there appeared a
par. in the Red-Page criticising a new magazine
called Bradley, His Book, published in America,
and ascribing it to Bradley who illustrated Melb.
Punch a few years ago. “His work on Punch,”
you say, “was generally clever and piquant, and
there were some artistic regrets when by-and-by
he drifted away into oblivion.” You are all out
m this item, confounding two Bradleys, Will
Bradley and L. D. Bradley, the former famous as
an artist, with an American and European reputa-
tion —he is particularly noted for his clever poster-
work. The magazine reviewed is hit,. He never
saw Australia. L. D. Bradley is the man who
drew for Punch. The latter is also an American,
but he did not “drift into oblivion” by any
means : he returned to Chicago where, as illustra-
tor of the Inter-Ocean, he is looming conspicu-
ously. A recent N. Y. mrgazine article places him
first amongst American provincial cartoonists.
Strange to say, he never put pen to Bristol-board
with the idea of making a picture till some time
after his arrival in Australia, when he was over
30. His work on Punch was the result of very
little experience. He had a cartoon or two in
The Bulletin, by the way.—Edward Dyson.
FRED. LANE, of E. Sydney Swimming Club,
N.S.W. amateur champion.
Photo., Brand.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.), who goes to
England shortly to be sworn in as a Privy Coun-
cillor, will not be satisfied with a Privy Counoil-
lorship and a seat as an Appellate Judge. What
little Sammy has been striving for is a salary from
combined Australia and residence in England.
Way is so ambitious that he has, as is well-known,
repeatedly refused knighthood as being insuffi-
cient. The erstwhile farm apprentice would pro-
bably only be satisfied with a dukedom and—
Samuel is a wealthy bachelor —marriage with a
princess.

In connection with the forthcoming
baptism of Captain Chirnside’s son and
heir, and the “ housewarming” of the
Manor house, it was mentioned in last
week’s issue that those privileged to par-
ticipate in the festivities, etc., would likely
receive special invitations. It seems, how-
ever, that it is the desire of Captain and
Mrs. Chirnside to welcome friends and
well wishers from all parts of the district
—even from distant parts like Little River.
All who wish to participate in the import-
ant and interesting event of Saturday, 6th
February, will be made heartily welcome;
that is to say, the invitation is a general
one to all, and not a special one to a

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. civil servants will almost to a man
leave out the Ministerial candidates for the
Federal Convention.
Premier Turner’s Federal Council pro-
posals show his knighthood to have already given
him an Imperialistic tinge.
The appointment of “ Bob ” Philp as
Acting-Treasurer of Q. during Premier Nelson’s
absence at Hobart is read as indicating that when
Nelson resigns he will advise Lamington to “ send
for Philp.”
The Bulletin would ask the self-termed
N.S.W. “ Patriotic Party,” which now holds up
Barton to vilification tor having prosecuted the
Broken Hill strikers, what they did towards pre-
venting that action ?
There is some political significance in
the fact that Kanakaland has struck Anniver-
sary Day off its official list of holidays—
probably with a view to substituting the birthday
of the King of the Cannibal Islands.
S.A. Tories have allowed their organ, the
Country, to die just on the eve of important Leg.
Council elections. The pending contests will de-
cide whether the slender Fat majority in the
Council is retained. From a stomach point of
view, the Country was a good, reliable political
liar, but it became particularly dull from the time
a certain Supreme Court Judge was compelled to
cease contributing political articles to its columns.
NOT NEEDLESSLY PARTICULAR.
SWAGGIE (sticking “ swell” up for an old pair of trousers): “Well, boss,
if yor ain’t got none, p’r’aps the missus goes a-biking and can
spare me a pair that’s out of fashion, for I ain’t partic’lar.”
In his recent speech at Sydney, the native-
born Mr. L. F. Heydon, M.L.C., said: “Our
country (N.S.W.) is so large and rich as to offer a
very sufficient object for our loyalty.” Now, N.S.
Wales is in area about one-ninth of Australia.
Mr. Heydon, therefore, is loyal to one-ninth of
his fatherland.
The Q.N. Bank Relief Act passed Q. Par-
liament only on Premier Nelson’s distinct pledge
that the smothered enquiry into the bank’s posi-
tion would be proceeded with. It is now an-
nounced that the enquiry has been further
smothered pending the bank’s arrangement with its
creditors. The Labor-members who belied their
past and their policy by trusting Boodlewraith are
vainly filling the air with reproaches.
The latest from New Australia. That
happy Socialistic community possessed two wells
■ —one of clear, sparkling water, the other running
through a clayey bank. Dictator Casey, believing
the latter supply better for the health of the com-
munity, removed the rope thither from the first
well. Hence grievous discord, revolution, and ap-
pointment of a new Despot.
Several Federal politicians tried to get
election proclamations into the Sydney dailies in
the guise of “letters to the editor.” But the busi-
ness managers had foreseen this, and the editors
were on guard. It was pathetic to read in Sydney
D.T. the other day, for instance, than an epistle
had been received from Mr. O’Blank, arguing
that so-and-so, etcetera, but the letter was “ too
long for publication.”
Dear old ex-Republican Dibbs says scorn-
fully that to build a new Federal capital would
cost .£250,000 a year, which, at 4 per cent., means
that the total cost would be £6,250,000. Con-
sidering that the Federal Government would get
the land either gratis or at prairie value, the
splashy Dibbs seemingly proposes that the State
should build a city to hold 30,000 people—build it
all, private houses included—and then let the
whole place rent free.
Rockhampton is still the leading port of
Bananaland in the matter of exports. The total
exports, however, show a falling off of .£250,000 —
decrease confined to the meat-trade. Centralia’s
wool export has increased by three million lb. in
weight, or £61,000 in cash. Gold shows also an
increase of 2000 oz. Query : If Gladstone were
connected with the Central line, would a couple of
wheelbarrows suffice to trundle Rockhampton’s
exports down to the Fitzroy ?
Agent-General and ex-Governor Norman
assures the bulls and bears of London that the new
Queensland loan is not for the purpose of propping
up the dreadful Q. N. Bank, but is to be expended
on “authorised public works.” But then any
kind of public works is “authorised” if the
Government majority chooses to authorise it, and
anything is a public work if Parliament says it is.
“ Authorised public works ” is a good, sonorous
phrase, but it means anything or everything or all
sorts of things.
Dear Bulletin, —
Do titles wreck Austra-
lian politicians, after
all ? Parkes was Pre-
mier twice before he got
his title and three times
afterwards, and his title
didn’t blast him, any-
how. Robertson was as
fortunate a politician
after he got his title as
before, barring that he
was older and poorer,
two things for which
his K.C.M.G.-ship was
in no way responsible.
Dibbs fell from office
after he took a title,
but he also fell twice
before he took it, and
he ruled longer as
Dibbs, K.C.M.G., than
as plain Dibbs. Plain
M‘llwraith was once
Premier of Queensland:
MTI wraith, K.C.M.G.,
was Premier twice.
Plain Samuel Griffith
was Premier for
years : Samuel Griffith,
K.C.M.G., was at the
head of affairs for 2f
years. Berry certainly
vanished out of politics
when he got his title,
but he didn’t vanish
more completely than
the democrat, Higin-
botham. Patterson with
a title didn’t crumble
any worse than Shiels
without one. Vogel had
a title almost all through
his meteoric career ; he
was Premier two years
as Vogel, C.M.G., and
virtual Premier three
years as Vogel,
K.C.M.G. ; when he
was plain Vogel he was
never more than a sub-
ordinate. Stout,
K.C.M.G, was Pre-
mier for three years;
plain Stout was never
more than an underling
in the very weak and
incapable Grey Cabinet.
Baronet O’Loghlen never came to the front at all
till he got his title. Which way does the balance
of evidence lie ? Certainly every titled politician
must fall some time; also every other politician.
—Z.
Maoriland now reckons that its surplus at
the end of the financial year will be close on
.£300,000. If so, the necessity for floating a one-
million loan and thus going back into the mire of
Vogelism isn’t very apparent. A country with
<£300,000 surplus should be able to crowd through
without borrowing.
The cry of the Sydney importer about the
league that will be formed in the Commonwealth
Parliament by S.A. and Victoria against N.S.W.
can only pass muster with the densely ignorant.
It implies that Vic. and S.A. have a common
interest—which they haven't, and never had.
S.A. demands a considerable strip of Victorian
territory on the ground of an error in the original
survey. When the border tariffs are abolished,
S.A. ports will be the natural outlet for about
one-fifth of Victoria, and a lot of business will
leave Melb. for Adelaide. The idea of Vic. and
S.A., with these two corpses between them, being
natural allies against anybody, is wildly ridiculous.
The N.S.W. Freetrade —smile here !
Government surreptitiously protects the cement
industry by accepting a local tender for 70,000
casks at 6d. a cask more than the importers price.
Therefore, the oppressed taxpayer pays £1750 to
support an exotic industry, and “ trade is diverted
from its legitimate channels,” and all the other
horrors of Protection arise on the scene.
THE ONLY CHILD—AGAIN.
(Room full of company.)
PROUD MA (leading in little Johnny, to proud father) : “ My dear, this hoy has been fighting
again. ”
PROUD FATHER: “ Pooh, pooh, my dear —I like to see my son able to take his own part.
What were you fighting about, Reginald? ”
REGINALD : “ Well, dad, Tommy Smith said he could whop me just as easy as mum whops
you, and I showed him he couldn’t .”
(That night someone whopped Johnny. It wasn’t his ma.)
W. P. Crick is probably the most remark-
able N.S.W. Federal candidate up to date. He
reports that he will be “no party to any act that
will abrogate or minimise the powers of the people
or Parliament of N.S.W.” If the Parliament of
N.S.W. is not going to give up any of its powers,
even to the Federal Legislature, then the latter
can’t have any powers at all, and, if it has no
powers, then it can only exist for the purpose of
playing dominoes and having free-passes and
drinks. Crick says he is also dead against “ Fede-
ration without conditions.” Who ever spoke or
dreamt of Federation without conditions? The
natural conditions are that every Australian shall
have, as far as possible, an equal share in the
Government, and in the making of laws ; shall live
under the same taxation system, and shall have
the same show as every other Australian.
Dr. Quick, of Bendigo (Vic. ), one of the
most conspicuous advocates of Federation, pub-
lishes his Federal election address on page 2 of
this issue. It is a good address in some points,
yet it isn’t nearly so good as The Bulletin
expected from such a source. Dr. Quick proposes
to federate part of the public debts only, which is
a weak half-measure. He says nothing about
depriving the provincial authorities of the borrow-
ing powers which they have so shamelessly mis-
used in the past, and of which they should have
no honest need in the future. He proposes that
the Commonwealth should have a “limited and
defined control over railways for Federal pur-
poses,” which sounds very differently from the ab-
solute Federalcontrol so urgently needed. He holds
that the States should retain all their powers except
those expressly transferred to the Federal authority
providing that the Federal authority
should hold all powers except those expressly re-
served to the States—which is quite another
thing. Also, he proposes that the provinces should
. federate indissolubly under the Crown ’’—which
is capable of two meanings. The Bulletin
believes that the provinces should federate indis-
solubly from choice, and under the Crown as a
temporary yielding to necessity. If Dr. Quick
means the same thing, this paper concurs. But if
he means that the Federation should be indis-
solubly connected with Britain, The Bulletin
isnt having any. In any case, he should say
exactly what he does mean, unless language is
?’ ven t° a Federal candidate to conceal his
thoughts.
As soon as N.S.W. Parliament meets some
member should move for a return of all moneys
paid by Government to M’sL.C. and M sL.A. in
the form of expenses on commissions and select
committees since Reid came into power. The
Select Committee on Abattoirs is only one, and
not the worst, example of the tipping of political
friends out of the public Treasury.
South Australia, on whose consent that of
Westralia is contingent, will withdraw her objec-
tion to the institution of Imperial penny-postage,
and so Henniker Heaton (who has the written
consent of Queensland and N.S. Wales) will soon
have his way. The rate from Australia to England
will, however, stand at 2£d. as at present.
The S.M. Herald’s latest anti-Federal
correspondent writes to point out that N.S.W.
should avoid all union with S.A. as it would a
serpent, because of that province’s “ selfishly
grasping ” management of the Broken Hill trade.
The selfish graspingness lies in the fact that S.A.
happened to have a railway which suited the con-
venience of the Broken Hill people, and as N.S.W.
refuses to build one of its own, the Hill has to use
the S.A. line or else shut up altogether. The S.A
railway management alone keeps N.S. W.’s biggest
mining-field from being abandoned, and L. F.
Heydon and others solemnly curse her for coming
to the rescue. This is a grateful world.
L. F. Heydon, M.L.C., announces as an
evidence of the deep awfulness of Federation that
one-third of N.S.W. is nearer to Melbourne
than to Sydney ; one-fourth is nearer to Adelaide
than Sydney, and one-fifth is nearer Brisbane than
Sydney. When these things are added up they
mean that one-half of N.S.W. is nearer to othei
ports than to Sydney. The fact that some of the
places alluded to are quite near Newcastle,
Grafton, &c., and that N.S.W. has lots of ports
besides Sydney, doesn’t enter into Heydon’s philo-
tl? o Heydon kind of man Sydney is
N.S.W. and the rest of N.S.W. is nothing in par-
ticular. Taking Heydon at his word, however,
\u E Proposes as an amendment that the
other half of N.S.W. is also nearer Brisbane than
Sydney, and that Sydney is nearer to Melbourne
than it is to itself, and that unless some arrange-
ment is made whereby Sydney will be as near to
Adelaide as Adelaide is to Sydney the latter city
will be Lost. 3

Up till last Friday, 31 Convention candi-
dates were announced in N.S.W.—eight lawyers,
one doctor, three merchants, three journalists, one
compositor, two miners, two “agents,” three
“gentlemen,” two secretaries, one plasterer, one
storekeeper, two mechanics, one carpenter, one
tailor. Victoria had 27 candidates—nine lawyers,
two civil engineers, one mining manager, one mer-
chant, one brewer, five “ gentlemen,” one doctor,
two booksellers, one bootmaker, one house-agent,
one mining-speculator, one compositor, and one
agent. Victoria is ahead both in lawyers and
“gentlemen.” The lawyers include some of the
best men of the province—Turner and Isaacs, for
instance —but the “ gentlemen ” are mostly to be
viewed with suspicion.

Election Notices-
Chief Secretary’s Office,
Sydney, 26th January, 1897.
IT is hereby notified for general information
that a Writ of Election has this day been
received by me for the return of Ten Representa-
tives to serve in the Convention of Australasia,
and that the following dates are the dates for
carrying out the election :
Date of Writ—26th January, 1897.

The up-country gelding Tom (Invader—Marian),
who won Anniversary Handicap at Rand wick after per-
forming ingloriously the previous Saturday, was origin-
ally bought for 17gs., but proving an inveterate buck-
jumper was put into a sulky, where he soon quieted
down; he afterwards became a regular up-country cham-
pion. On the advice of a leading N.S.W. jockey he was
brought to Sydney. Besides winning stakes and bets his
party sold him to Mr. Kelso for 300 gs.
• • • *
If racing is to be kept at all clean stewards
should investigate each and every case of sudden reversal
of form; and if occasion demands it they should have
the power to impound a horse and run him against the
winner at same weights. Many impostures would thus
be shown up, and terror struck into the camp of the
“waiting” division.
• * * *
Harvey, the owner of Bloodshot, dreams of
taking down Newhaven in a w.f.a. event at V.R.CJ.
Autumn Meeting. He will start the big-little horse (D.V.)
for the Australian Cup, Champion Stakes and Essendon
Stakes, and if the mighty Derby-winner tries to run away
with the latter event Bloodshot will endeavor to hang on
to his hind wheel.
• • * *
Survivor (Loohiel-Melissa) in carrying 9.5 to
victory in Flying Handicap at S.T.C. meeting, has proved
himself one of the best sprinters in N.S.W. With 7.12 he
had previously hunted Precaution home in Carrington
Stakes, and next day won New Year’s Gift, carrying 8.5.
He incurs no penalty for Newmarket, in which he carries
8.12, and should therefore go near winning.
* » » »
N.S. Wales’ San Marco (Niagara—Veronese)
has been sent to Vic., where he should pick up a few good
races, even if he does not star in the Newmarket. He is
very speedy, and well able to carry 8.2, which is his impost
in the big six furlong race.
* • • •
Ed. Bulletin, — Some little time ago I drew a
so-called favorite in one of Tattersall’s sweeps. Acting on
a hint, I offered to bet the owner £5OO to nothing about
his horse. Answer : “ Keep your £5OO for charitable pur-
poses.” Then I offered £l5OO to nothing, and got back
word that the horse would run. The horse ran, but was
not in the race—because I had not offered enough ? Mr.
Adams has the name for being a straight goer; can he
not stop the continual bleeding of his patrons by jockeys
and horse-owners ? —Sportsman.
* * * *
N.S.W. Income-tax Department has already
sent notices to winners of Tattersall’s sweeps during ’95
intimating that the tax must be paid on the amount they
received. This will come hard on the individual who is
the figurehead for a big syndicate, as, if his co-winners
will not pay, he will have to part up for the lot; but pos-
sibly the proportion paid the horse-owner will be allowed
off.
* • * •
Much of Sep. Miller’s apparent bad luck on the
turf is the natural result of a bad racing policy. The
wealthy chairman of the V.R.O. seems rather inclined to
have a large number of poorly-bred horses running on the
flat than a few really useful ones, and no straight-going
owner can expect to reap much satisfaction from a stable-
ful of platers. In the hurdle and steeple branch of sport
he shows more enterprise, and appears to get a better re-
turn for his money.
» * * •
The Heavens do not smile on the efforts of the
Boodlewraith to suppress gambling in the Northern pro-
vince. In the Rosehill Handicap Consultation first
money, £6750, went to Roma, and third money, £1125, to
Winton.
* * » •
The coming together of Messrs. Eales and
Gorrick in the pigeon-match last week—when Gorrick
won was worthy of a couple of America’s best
newspaper fighters. In a sporting paper published
the week previous to the match, Mr. Gorrick allegedly
said “ it struck him forcibly that Mr. Eales was not too
anxious to meet him ; ” whilst Mr. Eales allegedly replied
that “ he very much doubted whether Mr. Gorrick would
come up to the niark.” The bruisers have hitherto held
a monopoly of this sort of talk.
* * * *
In Melbourne last week, Charles Cavill stayed
under water smin. ssec., setting other cranks a world’s
record to try and beat. A swimmer of Cavill’s calibre
should not need any such monkey tricks to boom the art
of which he is such an able exponent.
* * * *
The recent Inter provincial sailing-races in Port
Jackson may be looked upon as an unqualified success—
barring, perhaps, the win of Flying Fish in the 22ft.
race on the last day. The F.F. is composed of two half
boats with a platform between, and the unfairness of
bringing half-decked, comfortable crafts from other pro-
vinces to find they are pitted against a catamaran or
sailing-machine is obvious. All the same, the sailing
capacity of the aforesaid Flying Fish is an eye-opener.
Golding’s two last creations —Vigilant (22ft.) and
Thalia (18ft.) —were probably the most consistent per-
formers at the meeting, although it must be said the races
showed as much reversal of form as a trotting or pony
meeting. Thalia, by running into places every event
showed herself to be an all-weather boat; and Vigilant
had the bad luck, on Anniversary Day, to spring her mast
when holding a comfortable lead, and, as her skipper was
afraid to jibe her, for fear of putting the stick over the
side, the Queensland Bulletin had the luck to score the
only win the province made during the series.
On the first day the Irex fairly sailed away with
the 22ft. race, but she did nothing afterwards. The sur-
f>rise of the meeting was the failure of Joe Donnelly’s
atest 22-footer, the Effie. After three consecutive wins
immediately after launching, her poor showing in the
carnival is not to be explained unless Mr. M'Murtrie made
some alteration in her trim or gear. A case in point was
the old yacht Alec a score of years ago. When launched
she proved a flyer, but Mr. Tom Brown altered her trim,
and he could never strike it again, for from that day till
she was converted into a steam tug she never won a race.
Fig-tree, 22ft., built by Stevens, is a likely cus-
tomer, but a blow is where she ought to show her best.
The pair built Dy young Deering, Wonga, 22ft., and Stella,
18ft., had terrible luck all through. Veteran George Ellis,
who ought to know, reckons the Stella about the best 18-
footer he ever sat in.
Frederick Lane, the 220yds. amateur swimming
champion of N.S.W., is a student at Sydney Grammar
School—a typical old boy. His acquisition of the title is
one of those streaks of fortune which are often mistaken
by the unthinking as a criterion of the man. Young-
Lane is a fine swimmer, and there is no getting over the
fact that he beat Hellings, Gormly and Cavill, but these
cracks were all stale through incessant training and
tough struggles with each other in previous contests this
season. Lane’s time was put down at 2min. 46sec., which
is 7sec. worse than the world’s record, but as he only just
beat the other men, and as these swam slowly, the time
must be accepted with portion of one eye closed. There
is plenty of promise in Lane, however, but he will have to
down the always-fighting three when they haven’t been
fighting each other to death before he can rank as a
champion. No branch of athletics continually followed
will make a man so stale as swimming does.
* * * *
The Rambler bicycle has lowered the price of its
first grade from £25 to £2l; and that of its second grade
from £l5 to £ll. *
The Federation Convention Election.
TO THE ELECTORS OF NEW SOUTH WALES.
Gentlemen,— Pursuant to previous announcement, I
intend offering myself as a candidate for your suffrages
at the forthcoming Federation Convention Election.
The scheme of Federation which I approve is one that
will give to the Federal Parliament the right to legislate
upon all subjects in which the people of Australia have a
common interest; and which will leave to the individual
States sovereign powers to deal with all matters not ex-
pressly delegated to the Federal Legislature.
The Federal Parliament, in addition to controlling Cus-
toms and Excise, and dealing with all matters appertain-
ing to defence, post and telegraphs, &c., should, I think,
control—virtually and actually—the railway policy of
Australia. There are at present two impediments to free-
dom of trade over the continent. Intercolonial Customs
barriers constitute one. The other is a railway policy
which compels people in the outlying districts of" a large
State to trade with the capital of their own State by ex-
pressly denying to them railway communication with a
point on the seaboard a hundred, perhaps two hundred,
miles nearer to them—merely because that point happens
to be the Capital of another State.
It is conceded on every hand that under Federation the
border tariffs must go. To abolish a border tariff to per-
mit freedom of trade between the colonies, and to main-
tain a railway policy that prevents it, is merely to mock
at the basic principle of commercial union, and to convert
Federation from a reality into a miserable make-believe.
If the individual States have absolutely sovereign rights
in respect of all matters except those expressly delegated
to the Federal Parliament, it seems to me that federal
legislation should represent the will of a majority of the
people of the Federation, and not a majority of mere
green and red and yellow divisions painted on a map.
The principle of majority will be seriously endangered if
the Upper House of the Federal Legislature, possessing
an absolute power of veto, is composed, as the Common-
wealth Bill proposes, of an equal number of senators from
each State, irrespective of population. Under such a
system, a majority of people might, for an indefinite
period, be kept at bay by a minority. And the strength
of this minority would arise from the extraordinarily in-
consequential fact that their residence-places were scat-
tered over a larger number of map-divisions than were
those of the majority.
At least one of the official leaders of the Federal move-
ment has intimated that unless the principle of equal re-
presentation in the senate be conceded, there can be no
federation. He accordingly proposes to concede it • but
at the same time, suggests an expedient that makes the
concession utterly worthless in practice. If it be regarded
as advantaging anyone to make a formal concession to the
principle of “ State rights,” I am perfectly willing to make
it—on condition that the constitution provides some means
whereby the will of the majority of people, as expressed
hy their representatives, may ultimately, and without un-
due delay, prevail. In other words, I am prepared to give
a “ majority of States ” the shadow of supreme authority
so long as a majority of the people of united Australia re-
tains the substance.
Election of the Senators by the people, as against their
selection by the State Legislatures, I am, of course in
favor of. ’
The site of the Federal Capital—a question which has
little material interest for the great mass of toiling men
and women, but apparently much for the higher ranks of
society—I would leave to the Federal Parliament for
determination.
My views on the various principal points of the federa-
tion problem are enunciated more fully than above in the
P. ap ? rs of 25th Jan ' Briefl .V outlined, my position
is that I desire to see set up a Constitution which will
enable the people of Australia to act unitedly in all mat-
ters of large national concern; which will prevent the
Government of one State pirating the commerce that
naturally belongs to another State, and which will
enable the States—as, for instance, in such a
matter as debt-consolidation—to secure by co-ODera
tion advantages which, singly, they could not obtain
for themselves The ideas which I have expressed here
and elsewhere I believe to make in that direction • and I
have been moved to restate them by an utter want
of sympathy with the spirit of narrow localism which
is now being fostered in Sydney by men who are appar
ently quite ready to smother a nation at its birth in order
that, as they suppose, the interests of some pet puny
weakling of their own may not be prejudiced. * B
It is my intention to deliver addresses on early dates at
Bathurst, Orange, Dubbo, Bourke, Newcastle Maitland
Tamworth, Armidaie, Goulburn, Wagga, Alburv Denffil
quin and Broken Hill, and in the city and suburbs
If elected, I will devote the whole of my time and
energies to the performance of the work entrusted tome
I am your obedient servant.
30th January, 1897.
JAS. ASHTON.

Sheets furnished by the various branches, the latter being certified to by the respective Managers and

correct view of the affairs of the Bank as shown by the books on the 31st December, 1896.

Is This a Miracle?
AN M.P’s VIEWS ON THE SUBJECT.
WHAT MR. LABOR-LEADER M‘GOWEN
KNOWS.
From the Australian Workman.
Mr. J. S. T. M'Gowen, leader of the Labor-
party in the N.S.W. Assembly, was seen by our
reporter in connection with the remarkable case of
Mr. .Thomas Jarvis which was given some weeks
ago in this paper and which is given below. Mr.
M'Gowen says he has known Jarvis from infancy.
He also knew that he had to give up his work at
Glebe Island abattoirs twelve months ago, owing
to a malady which he developed. He afterwards
heard that Jarvis was bedridden, and that there
was little chance of his recovery. He understood
Jarvis was refused admission to the Sydney Hos-
pital, as his case had been pronounced incurable.
Mr. M‘Gowen was, therefore, like everyone else
who knew the facts, astounded at the complete re-
covery of Jarvis.
THOMAS JARVIS INTERVIEWED.
On Thursday afternoon, our representative
made his way to the humble home of Mr. Thomas
Jarvis, 45 Crown-lane, Ultimo. He was discovered
nursing a two year-old baby, and his face declared
him to be the happiest man in Sydney. He was
up and about and able to answer the knock at the
or ‘ . pale and bearing still the marks
of a critical illness, and a period of pain, it was
very clear that he was an invalid on the rapid road
to convalescence.
(t reply to the preliminary questions, he said :
You want to know all about my case ? Well, I
am glad of an opportunity to tell you. The facts
may be of value to those who are stricken as I
was.”
“ How long were you suffering?”
‘‘Well, twelve months ago I got so bad that I
had to leave off work and I have done nothing
since then. About five years ago, next Christmas,
I met with an accident; I was badly scalded
while taking a bath in Pitt-street, Sydney. Since
the accident I have been in failing health, and
after four years of gradual development of the
malady, I was forced to give up my billet at the
Glebe Island.”
“ What were the symptoms ? ”
“ Simply indescribable. The pains were some-
thing terrible. They were just about as much as
I could bear. They were for all the world like as
if a saw were cutting my bones in two in every
part of me. I had no control over my bowels or
bladder, and was a nuisance to myself and every-
body else. I was incessantly shivering and shak-
ing, and could neither eat nor sleep. On the 23rd
March last I saw a well-known doctor and he told
me that my case was utterly hopeless. However,
he gave me an order to the Director of Charitable
Institutions.”
“ Did you make use of the order ? ”
“No ; I came home and went to bed, and have
been pretty well in bed ever since. Five weeks
ago I could not walk across the floor; in fact, I
could not get out of bed. I felt then that it was
all over with me, and only a question of days when
I would give up the ghost. My doctor was at-
tending me, and did all he could, by opium and
morphia pills, to ease the agony I was in. I was
under him for five or six weeks. He came to the
conclusion that I was suffering from locomotor
ataxia, or progressive locomotor ataxy, a disease
of the spinal cord, characterised by peculiar dis-
turbances of gait, and difficulty in co-ordinating
voluntary movements. He told me that I might
last for ten years, and that I might never get the
use of my legs. (Just then the patient kicked his
legs about with energy to show that medical
opinion is not infallible.) Well, just as a drown-
ing man will grasp at a straw, I grasped at an
idea conveyed by the advertisement describing the
cure effected in the case of another boiler-maker
in England, which I read in the Australian
Workman, by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. I read in the advertisement of a case
which seemed to fit my own. I determined to
give the Pink Pills a trial anyway, and sent for a
box.. The effect was as marvellous as it was
magical. I was able to eat and sleep as though
nothing was the matter with me. It was the first
appetite and rest I had for many a day. It
was incredible. I immediately left the bed and
was able to move about. I have taken four boxes
of Pink Pills during the past four weeks, and am
now taking the fifth box. I can walk about with
the aid of a stick, and am gradually, but surely re-
covering my former vigor and vitality. You see
those crutches there? They are perfectly new,
and I have never used them. They were made
for me before I got the Pink Pills, but I was too
weak to use them and now I don’t want them.
All I want is this stick, and I hope soon to do
without it. You see I am rapidly regaining my
strength.”
“ He is 100 per cent, better,” said Mrs. Jarvis,
who had come in and taken the baby on her knees.
“The Pink Pills have saved his life and made
another man of him.”
A friend who called in at this point, said that
Jarvis looked 10 years younger that day than he
did a fortnight previously.
“lean now,” continued Jarvis, “have a cold
bath, and all the feeling has come back in my legs.
If I keep on going the way I am, I hope to be able
to resume work as a boiler-maker, and you know I
have to be in the very pink of health and strength
to work at that trade. lam better to-day than I
have been at any time during the past five years.
I ascribe all the change and benefit to these Pink
Pills. They have saved my life. lamin no pain
now, whatever, and feel like a new man. On the
mantelpiece there is a box of opium and morphia
pills which the doctor gave me to deaden the ex-
cruciating pam. But they can stay where they
are, for Ino longer need them. Everybody who
knows me has been amazed at the change in me.
They can hardly believe their eyes. My being
about again has caused quite a sensation out here. ’

Broken Hill varied its awful monotony of
sunstroke and red dust by a gruesome New Year
sensation. A resident returned to his wooden
residence when the holidays were over, and feeling
his way in the dark became vaguely aware of
something dead. He lighted the lamp, and then
the sensation of something dead became so over-
whelming that he left hurriedly and went to have
tea with a friend. Later on he returned with a
reinforcement and investigation revealed a de-
ceased citizen under the floor in a condition about
which the less said the better. At the inquest i
doctor reported that deceased had a rare and won
derful complaint which would ultimately tend tc
drive him mad with a peculiar form of madness, in
which the patient always wanted to shove himself
lengthwise into a hole and die there. Several
witnesses reported having met an unusual sort of
maniac who drank immense quantities of water,
and yapped at them in a peculiar fashion. Anothei
man reported having captured the unusual maniac
and taken him to the hospital, but he was refused
admission, there being no demand for curios just
then. Consequently he was turned loose and went
away to yap some more and drink more water.
Verdict: death from natural causes. There are
so many strange ways of dying in the land of dust
and grey smoke and lead-fumes that they take
sensations peacefully there.

INVALIDS
( AND
The aged.
A correspondent condemns the new Sydney
letter-boxes. Not only are they too small, but,
becoming congested on holiday periods (or by
Sunday nights) they are liable to periodical rob-
bery, and special facilities are afforded for the
larrikin game known as “ shouting the post-office
pipe a smoke.” In the old pillars the chances
were that an inserted match would extinguish
itself in its downward course. It is further sug-
gested that Sydney letter-carriers should be fur-
nished with straps in lieu of twine for securing
their packages.
While Triple Alliance confronts Dual
Alliance, and Britain shivers in her splendid
isolation, and the U.S. is being lashed up to fight
Spain for Cuba, the mammoth intellect of the
Manning River Times man, who of old used to
lash out at Bismarck and Schouvaloff, silences the
clamours of a shrieking universe with a leader on
“ Grass in the Streets.”

Jules Joubert has got his little Interna-
tional Exhibition scheme in going order in Bris-
bane (Q.). The affair was supposed to be dead
three months ago. The then “ directors ” dis-
pensed with his services. Joubert, in his very
best broken English—he always adopts broken
English when he is executing his master-
strokes —said (in effect): “You dees-sharje, ah,
me? No! I, ah, dees-sharje, ah, you! Zis ees
not your exheebeeshiong ! Eet ees of, ah, me !'
Then he rounded up another set of directors, anc
worried the Government to grant him a subsidy
“I, ah, want five zousand pounds i ” The
Government jibbed, but it franked his official
letters, and permitted him to use the “ Royal
arms” on his stationery. “ I vhill get zat five
zousand pounds ! ” said Joubert; “ eet vill come
all right.” Later, the Govt, applied for space fox
exhibits. “ Zere ees no space to spare. You
want an annexe? Ver’ good. Ze price of ze
annex is five zousand pounds.” And the betting
is that Government “ won’t subsidise the exhibi
tion, oh, dear no ! It will merely pay for space.’
Also, since the Government has put its imprima-
tur upon the exhibition, and made it a sort of
State affair, it is pledged in a manner to ensure its
success. Joubert is a smart man.

DISCOVERER AND PROPRIETOR

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfahne Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in.every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and No.‘2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “ Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ! Ihe
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

mHIS COMPANY is being formed for the purpose of purchasing G.M. Leases Nos. 43x, 426 x, and
x > * n the North-East Coolgardie Goldfield, containing a total area of 36 acres, and situated
about 2$ miles N.E. from Kanowna and about 14 miles from Kalgoorlie.
An immense lode formation from 180 ft. to 200 ft. in width has been proved right through the three
leases, which gives the handsome return of over 2oz. to the ton, whilst the cost of treating the ore
should not exceed lOdwt. per ton.
Special attention is drawn to the reports, all of which are made by thoroughly reliable and capable
men of high standing.

Some years ago, in the Northern Territory,
a Chinaman raked in about 60oz. alluvial and
started back for Port Darwin. On the road,
somewhere between Rum jungle and Tumbling
Waters, he fell sick and lay down beside the
track. Other Chinese going along the track
passed over on the other side, like, the Levite.
(Chinamen nearly always forsake a sick country-
man—on the road, anyway.) By-and-by he died,
and by-and-by some teamsters along the road
threw some dry sticks on him and applied a match,
and so he was beautifully cremated. Not long
afterwards it leaked out that he had had about
60oz. of gold on him, and two white miners down
on their luck started from the Howley diggings,
found the remains, and panned them off for 30oz.
So, at least, a cheerful liar tells The Bulletin,
and maybe this time he hasn’t lied quite so much
as usual.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The circumstance that Q. authorities had
to send up North a commissioner to enquire into
Meston’s charges re ill-treatment of blacks, recalls
the fact that one of the most amazed men south of
the line appeared some time ago in a Q. police
court, and asked faintly what had happened. It
was up on the north-eastern coast, where the
nigger is still plentiful; and the parties to the
transaction were a squatter and a most hopelessly
bashed and bruised aboriginal. The white man
explained the situation affably. He had lost a
bullock, or something, and after scouring the
country in a dozen different directions he had
failed to find the animal, but had discovered a son
of the soil instead, sitting under a tree. When he
demanded the instant return of the vanished pro-
perty, the native had started to run, which was a
suspicious circumstance and almost positive evi-
dence of guilt. Therefore he had captured him, and
having tied him to a tree had flogged him in a most
profuse and lavish fashion in order to elicit infor-
mation, and, as no information was forthcoming,
he had fastened his hands and feet together, and
carted him into town on a charge of cattle-stealing.
If the bench would kindly send him to gaol right
off he would be obliged, as he wanted to get back.
Then the bench smiled a smile, and said it would
send him to gaol instantly. TheP.M. was admin-
istering justice by himself that day—the batch of
squatting J.’sP. who usually jugged each other s
niggers without asking any questions being all
absent; and he seemed out of sorts, for, as he
uttered this last remark, he made a strange crunch-
ing sound with his teeth, but the plaintiff set
this down to absent-mindedness. Then he started
to write something in a book, and, when he had
finished, laughed again and repeated in an un-
accountable sort of way that he would send him to
gaol without the slightest delay. The sentence
would be three months’ hard labor for assault.
Here the plaintiff rose and thanked the court
cordially, and remarked that though, the charge
was cattle-stealing it really made no difference, as
a sentence for assault would answer exactly the
same purpose ; but the Bench smiled its smile. over
again, and said gently that it was the plaintiff
himself who had got three months for a fiendish
attack on a defenceless nigger, combined with
illegal arrest, and an attempt to corrupt a court of
justice. Then, while the plaintiff glared at
vacancy a policeman collared him and dragged
him away. The last thing he saw as he left the
court was the P.M. still smiling, and then he sud-
denly doubled up at the knees, and asked the con-
stable if they were both mad, or if they were dead
and had arrived unexpectedly in hell; and when
he got no answer, but was simply shoved into a
whitewashed cell and left there, he. sat down
heavily on the floor, and moaned that it was evi-
dently all a dream.

The ruling passion. Norseman (W. A.) Miner
thus prints a recent cablegram : —“ Baroness Hirsch,
widow of the great philanthropist, has donated £BO,OOO
towards the erection of a hospital for children suffering
from exemption.” But who can wonder ! Exemption is
a terrible disease, and Westralia has got it very bad just
now.
* * * ♦
The more it is interpreted by the Devil’s Bri-
gade the more does Vic. Licensing Act become a dark and
fearsome mystery. The other day a Melb. publican was
hauled up on two charges of Sunday trading, the alleged
offences occurring on the same day. A conviction meant
pretty well wiping out the licensee, but his lawyer pointed
ont that, according to his reading of the Act, only one
fine could be imposed—the offender could only be con-
victed once for trading on the one Sabbath, no matter
how many times a breach might occur during said 24
hours. The defence was allowed, but the prosecutor has
given notice of appeal, on which momentous issues
depend.
* * * *
Salting is not quite unknown on Coolgardie. A
lease —adjoining a good one—on which was once a rich
alluvial deposit, was lately inspected with a view to pur-
chase, and a couple of bags of the formation taken as a
sample. These were sealed and left at the hotel where the
expert was staying, and, while he was absent for a short
time the bags were got at: a marlin-spike was inserted into
the side of the bag and a clean hole made, into which was
blown through a “ paper screw ” enough alluvial gold to
spoil the attempt. The bags were then worked about
and the contens mixed up, the hole closed itself. When
the contents were panned off the yield was such as to con-
firm the expert’s suspicions, with the result that he never
went near the mine again. But the salters know that the
expert knows, and are lying low.
* * * *
A recent large robbery of genuine securities
from a Melb. merchant’s office fetches to mind an odd
affair in Collins-street at the boom zenith. Some burglars
raked round in Mr. Boomster’s office, and the leading
journal said next morning how lucky they missed a
million pounds—in securities.
. * * *
Every mining spec, the mammoth composition
man has touched during the last 12 months has turned
up trumps, and he is now better off than ever. Another
“penny-in-pounder” has acquired marketable shares
(coal mainly) worth many thousands, whilst Melbourne
Stock Exchange would be almost without investors, were
insolvents “ comparrangementers,” and “settlement on
wifers ” debarred from speculating on it.

A North Queensland bankrupt, the amount of
whose liabilities requires five figures to express, and the
expected pan-out of which is 9d. in the £, had in the
fly leaf of his cash-book, the Scriptural legend— Diligent
in business, fervent in spirit, serving the Lord ihe
ledger fly-leaf showed—“ Seek ye first the kingdom of
heaven, and all those things shall be added unto you.
* * * *
Q Premier Nelson has authority to treat with
theQ.N. Bank on behalf of Queensland in connection
with the proposed “agreement” under the hurriedly-
passed Act; but the rumor, recently strongly current in
Brisbane, that it will be necessary for Nelson to go to
England on Q.N. Bank business may be contradicted with
safety. The British shareholders have arranged to send
out to Queensland two or more London accountants of
the highest standing, and those experts are to investigate
the bank’s affairs thoroughly. The life, or death, or
reconversion of the institution will depend upon this
report. . , , .
In connection with many of the central sugar-
mills established in Queensland, there are small farms of
40 to 100 acres that have been sold on terms to intending
cane-growers, whose names are placed on the co. s list.
The hoodwinked country naturally wants to keep these
small men “ living,” but doesn’t know that these cane-
growing farms are mortgaged to the big man, and that
the “sale” price-or “ terms ’’-covers land mortgage,
interest, and a fat profit besides. In this way the central-
mill svstefn is helping otherwise bankrupt institutions to
unload on to poor men unliquid properties at a boom
price with ruinous conditions attached.
, * * *
The Adelaide telegraph-operator scandals a,re
not surprising. When Govt, pays married men (with
families) £lOO a-year, after years of service, it must expect
leakages. “ A man must live.” Years ago, a similar leak-
age occurred, and the sympathy of the public was with
the dismissed men, who simply supplemented their starva-
tion salaries by standing-in with outside brokers and
specking on “ exclusive information.”
* » « *
A leading Adelaide sharebroker affirms that,
under a pledge of secrecy, a telegraph official, who could
have done what he said, offered, for a consideration, to
supply him with copies of all share telegrams from W.A.
two hours before they were delivered. Another broker
added that in Coolgardie the telegraph officials know the
codes used by every member of the exchanges in that
place.
* a * *
A Westralian magnate was asked to furnish a
report by a suitable man, and instructed one of his mine-
managers to make the report, and to charge (say) 100
guineas. When the fee was paid over, the magnate calmly
appropriated half of it for himself as his share. Fact.
* * * *
Keenly alive to the exaltedness of their posi-
tions, the mining engineers connected with the big com-
panies on the Boulder line (writes a scoffer to Thf. Bulle-
tin from Hannan’s) are about to have a railway-carriage
fitted up in sumptuous style for their own exclusive use.
In no part of the world is this sort of thing carried much
further than here. In a recent speech a prominent Aus-
tralian bishop described the leading Australian character-
istic as a want of reverence and awe. His reverence has
evidently never been in Westralia and witnessed the
homage paid to the uncrowned lode-kings—mostly ex-
jam-boilers, tailors and barbers, &c. It is only natural
that they should want a railway-carriage to themselves,
so that when travelling they may have that respect paid
to them which is their due ; but as when they leave the
carriage people, unaware of their rank and majesty,
may offer undue familiarities, it would be well for them to
wear tin hats and be accompanied by Jap. servants in
livery.
* * * *
“ Talking about notes, and being reminded of
the recently-exposed stamp-obliteration frauds in Mel-
bourne : When bank-notes become too dirty and tattered,
they are cancelled as they are received into the bank
again, and stand away until a pile has accumulated, when
they are destroyed 'in the bank-furnace. A Southern
bank-official once managed to get away with an old-man
bundle of cancelled notes. Like the Melbourne stamp-
artist, he treated them with chemicals, until he found an
ao-ent that obliterated the cancellation. Then he
managed to have then) distributed far and wide over the
country. Some time elapsed before any of them came
back to head-quarters ; a still longer time passed before
they attracted attention. There was a hubbub, and
much pow-wowing, but they never got enough evidence
for a prosecution.”

now saleable at over par.

Hint of strong competition has given the Vic-
torian explosive ring the shivers. The crowd has nad a
fine picking for some time, and opposition would be a
welcome relief and gain to the industry. As many co s
spend from £1500 to £2000 a year in explosives, a small
droop in prices would mean an appreciable saving.

* * * *
“ The rapid extension of ‘ incandescent-gaslight-
ing' ” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ necessitates a large and continual supply of thurium,
earth-metal discovered by Berzelius in thurite. The cone
curtain of the incandescent gas-jet is soaked in a solu-
tion of thurium, which renders it comparatively incom-
bustible and is responsible for the snow-white bright
li„ht emitted. At present the thurium supplies come
from the seashore sands of Brazil, but there is n
reason why Australasia should not contest this market
monopoly. Thurium has been found in small quantities
in the beds of Tasmanian gullies or creeks by searchers
for precious stones, but no serious attempt has been
made to procure regular supplies of a payaffie quantity.
Thurium is not likely to fall in value, so the attention of
the miner and prospector might be aroused in its quest.

In the inner circles of Brisbane (Q,) steamship-
ownerism (copyright) there was lately a whisper
that a cable had been received from the ditto ditto of
England that the latter could guarantee to shl P
tificated engineers to Australia within six weeks fioi

In at least one Melb. bank, clerks receiving ,£96
a year have often to work from 9 a.m. to 11 p.in., often
also on Saturdays and Sundays. Juniors receiving 1:30
must do the sanie, without tea-money and without com-
pensation of any kind—not time-off.”
London Referee adds its rough-and-ready argu-
ment to the stock of “Bimetallism” literature. “An
ounce of silver now fetches only half the price relatively
to gold that it did formerly, and it is ever getting cheaper.
The silverites demand that it shall be restored to its old
position in regard to gold, but that is beyond the power
of Bryant, or any other potentate on earth, for if every-
body could buy silver bars at half-a-crown an ounce, and
have them coined into money representing five shillings
an ounce, of course nobody would dream of paying his
debts in gold. The idea of the bimetallists that dear gold
would continue interchangeable with cheap silver is one of
the most amazing delusions that the world has ever
known.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why did Sir Henry Norman refuse the
Indian Viceroyship ? When on his journey from
Herberton to Cairns he stopped at a wayside
house about eight miles from Mareeba. At the
d<oor was an old follow who saluted the Governor
in approved military style. “To what regiment
did you belong?” “To the same as yourself
when you were plain Captain Norman in 1854,„
said the old fellow, cheerfully. “Your name?
“ John Jonathan Cole, your Excellency, and Sir
Henry remembered him, with great astonishment,
and shook hands fervently with his old war worn
friend of the 2nd Company Scotch 1 usileer
Guards. During their subsequent conversation,
Cole asked Sir Henry why he did not accept the
Viceroyship. And the usual sharp-eared, inquis-
itive person overheard Sir Henry reply, “ Not
from Gladstone, Cole ! ”
* * * *
Two Ms.L.A. mentioned in Cole’s hearing
that they had dined with Sir Henry “ I dined
with • him long before you fellows had left ott
knickerbockers,” observed the old warrior. One
morning, in the Crimea in ’54, Captain Norman,
when walking round the camp, found Cole roast-
ing a slice of bacon on the end of a ramrod.
“ That smells good, Cole! 5 4 * Better stop and
have some, Captain Norman. 55 And the future
General and Governor sat down and had a rasher
of bacon, a biscuit and a cup of coffee. As a
soldier he seems to have been at all times a great
favorite with his men, just as he was with all
classes of Queensland.
• * * *
Cardwell is a town on the shores of Rock-
ingham Bay. It is about the size of that Spanish
town where two women and a goose made a market.
The only break in the monotony of existence is a
cyclone that comes along, carries away one-halt
of the buildings, and fills the remainder up with
sand. A well-known local celebrity, one of the
oldest inhabitants, informed me confidentially :
“ I done pretty well here, though I nearly lost
everything in two o’ them dam saloons !
• * * *
Also, an unsophisticated old squatter from
the Upper Herbert had been on a visit to the
Johnstone River and saw a schooner full of 1 oly-
nesians, a race to which he has a strong objection,
tie remarked to me : “ This country will simply
go to blazes if the people allows whole shiploads
of them blasted ‘ politicians ’ to come here like
that ! Better let sugar slide ! ”
* * * *
When old Father M £ Nab was missionary
among the blacks at Durundur, he was one day
arranging their future occupations. One was to
be a carpenter, one a blacksmith, one a stockman,
&c., &c,, and an old gin with a vivid sparkle of
expectation in her dark eyes, eagerly enquired,
“ Wich one public houth ? ”
• * * *
The first State prosecution of a newspaper
in Queensland was against the late Theophilus
Pugh, as editor of Brisbane Courier, for a lead-
ing article on the Upper House, Bring was At-
torney-General, and the case was tried before the
late Justice Lutwyche. The fact that the Judge
himself had written the article may partly account
for the Courier being acquitted without a stain
on its character.
* * * *
A western Hibernian magistrate on the
Bench for the first time. He is brother of an
M.L.A. Before him is an old cadger named
O’Day, charged with “riotous conduct.” The
J.P. fixed him with his glistening eye. “Do you
know, sorr, that I cud give yez six pounds for this
offince ? ” “ I’ll take it ! ” said O’Day, cheerfully.
The clerk whispered to the Bench “ bix months,
your worship.” “Oi mane I cud give ynz SlX
months 1 ” “ I can’t take that , ’ said O Day,
despondingly.
*,* * *
A gin on a well-known Western station
has conducted a series of interesting ethnological
experiments. She has children by white men,
aboriginals, Kanakas, Chinese, and some other
race not yet clearly defined. Was it not Jupiter
and Juno who disputed as to whether marriage
brought most joy to the wife or the husband, and
consulted Tiresias—who had been one-haK of his
life a man and the other half a woman Ihe
Western gin is evidently endeavoring to settle
some equally subtle problem, and the repoit on
her favorite “ Benjamin ’’ is awaited with pro-
found interest.

Lady Norman was one day talking to a
representative Brisbane society woman who knows
nothing about law or lawyers, and not very much
about anything else. Reference was made to a
famous case then on at the Supreme Court. R
will be a very expensive case,’ said D.JN. ; 1
hear two of the barristers receive 30 guineas daily
for what they call ‘ refreshers ’! ” Society woman
looked shocked and observed, “What beastly
drunkards they must be ! ”
* * * *
At a picnic-party on Fraser’s Island a
young man who parted his hair in the middle was
present with a camera on tripod stand. (The day
cannot be far off when killing amateur photo-
graphers will be regarded as justifiable homicide.)
The original inhabitants were represented by one
old blackfellow with a spear. The A.P. decided
to give a wild bush aspect to the scene by telling
the o.b. to poise his spear “ all same kill black-
fellow ” when he gave the signal. The old chap
misunderstood the order, and, when it came, his
eye flashed with an ancient war-fire and he let go
the spear at the camera, the operator and the
instrument narrowly escaping impalement, lne
camera man wanted to have the Myall warrior
shot dead on the spot, but the picnickers smiled
until no one had strength enough to kill a sparrow.

Wool, Ho!
THE SONG OF “OUR STAPLE PRODUCT.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the clipper fleet comes over,
When the scent is on the clover,
And the scarlet streaks the blue ;
When the Western sheds are ringing
And the Western men are singing
As the toiling teams come through,
Then it’s ho, ho —Wool, ho !
For the busy shears are clipping, and a stir is in
the shipping,
And it’s yo, ho—Wool, ho !
When the boys have got together,
In the warm October weather,
When the tempest of their laughter
Shakes the hut from floor to rafter,
And the bush is turning brown ;
When the lover gets his maiden,
When the Southern teams are laden,
And the clip is rplling down,
Then it’s ho, ho —Wool, ho !
For the trucks are at the siding and the railway
men are chiding,
And ’tis go, go—Wool, ho !
Get that steamer to her berth there !
Get the men of all the earth there !
Have those lorries in their places !
Have the breeching to the traces ;
Get your wool-hooks fixed to heave.
Get your truck-wheels good and greasy;
Let the lower shoot run easy.
Have the guy-rope through the sheave !
Hey ! ho, the Wool, ho !
Hitch your belt until she pinches : is there steam
up on the winches ?
Go, go—Wool, ho !
Have the store-men got their muster
Ere the boss begins to bluster,
When the winch-men start reversing,
And the stevedore starts cursing,
And the wharf “ stands by ” below?
For she’s bound to sail by Monday—
“ Wool aboard ” at midnight, Sunday—
Wool, ho ! Wool, ho !
Oh ! the hungry looms are calling and the markets
may be falling—
Wool, ho ! Wool, ho!
Are ye ready ? Are ye ready ?
Heave aboard now ! Steady, steady !
Let them stand below the slings there,
Let them catch it as it swings there,
And their trust be in the Lord ;
For her skipper’s making trouble,
And the crowds are working double,
And it fairly hums aboard !
Does the Wool, ho ! the Wool, ho !
You ! the agent’s clerk is growling, and the for-
ward hatch is howling,
For their Wool, the Wool, ho !
You can ring the bell for dinner :
We have shoved her cargo in her,
And the Blazer mustn’t beat her
Though he’s flying his Blue Peter—
Hark ! you hear the hissing steam.
Now, she’s straining at her tether;
Now, they’re swinging out together
With their noses to the stream,
And the Wool! the Wool, ho !
Yes ! we cursed her and we damned her, but we’ve
rammed her and we’ve crammed her,
With the Wool! the Wool, ho !
Oh, we slung it and we slammed it,
And we squeezed it home and jammed it
For the glory of the Trade —
For her agent and her skipper,
And the comfort of the clipper
And the Broker-man’s Brigade.
Oh, ho, the Wool, ho ! the Wool, ho !
It will fill their pockets full, ho ;
It will buy their fowl and truffles and their ladies’
lace and ruffles,
Will the gritty, greasy Wool, ho !
Will the dirty, dunnaged Wool, ho !
With ten thousand bales of plunder,
And a cable’s length asunder ;
With a shouting and a cheering,
With the harbor pilot steering,
They are flopping down the bay.
She’s a ripper ! she’s a racer !
But the Blazer’s got to pace her,
And he’ll do it—all the way !
With the Wool! ho, the Wool, ho !
With his engineers and stokers and his able-bodied
And the Wool! the Wool! Wool, ho J
While her grinding engine’s grieving,
In the rolling and the heaving ;
While the sogging seas are swirling
With the white-capped surges curling
She will thunder on her way :
With her piston rods a-thumping,
While her heavy bows are jumping
Like a porpoise at liis play.
And the Wool, ho ! oh, the Wool, ho !
She will rise and buckle to it, she will chew a
roadway through it,
For the Wool! for the Wool! Wool, ho !
As her blocks aloft are creaking,
As her steam-escape is shrieking,
In the rising and the falling
Hear the bo’s’n’s whistle calling
When she strips to face the gale !
With the long green track before her,
With the storm-clouds black’ning o’er her
In the waning starlight pale. •
But the Wool, ho ! oh, the Wool, ho !
When she’s rolling and she’s lifting there will be
no cargo shifting—
For ’tis Wool, ho ! London brokers’ Wool, ho !
With an albatross to guide her,
While the dolphins race beside her,
When the restless screw is churning
And the blue a-wake is turning—
As she surges on—to cream ;
As the smoke shoots from her funnel,
As the shaft rings thro’ the tunnel,
As the sea-birds wheel and scream ;
With her Wool, ho ! the Wool, ho !'
She will romp and roll and toss it; fetch her
cargo safe across it—
For ’tis Wool, ho ! the Wool, ho !
When the vintage time is nearing,
When the corn ripes in the clearing,
When its scent has left the clover,
When the summer days are over,
And the South wind heads to rain ;
With a rolling swing to larboard,
With a swinging roll to starboard,
She’ll be clamping down again
For the Wool, ho !
With her goaded engines grieving thro’ the pitch-
ing and the heaving,
For the Wool, ho !
When the bees have stored their honey,
When the boys have spent their money
Ere the shears have started clipping,
Ere the stir is in the shipping,
She’ll be romping down the track ;
With the long green road before her,
With the bright stars beaming o’er her,
Rolling, rolling, rolling back
For the Wool, ho ! the Wool, ho !
Crowding ev’ry stitch she’s got on for the wool
that buys our cotton —
For the yellow, greasy Wool, ho !
The Wool, ho !
Ho, ho ! The blessed holy Wool, ho !
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
Found Wanting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You had better go,” she said. “My husband
will be in soon, you know, and he still believes in
you.”
“And in you?” The sneer in the man’s tone
sent the color to her cheeks.
“ Yes—in me, too, and he shall have the reason
for it in future which may have been wanting in
the past.”
“ May have been ? You are singularly guile-
less ! ”
She made no answer, but met his gaze. He
shrugged his shoulders and went on :
“Remember, I have been your cavalier at every
social function ; you have allowed me three, four,
on one occasion six, dances ; and—really there is
rarely smoke without fire. I have sent you flowers,
I have your photographs, many notes —sweet
little things some of them. Gossip spreads ; why,
at the club only yesterday— ”
“ What did they dare say there ? ”
“ Oh, nothing definite, just the vague things
that make rumor.”
She laughed, leant back in her chair, looked
him full in the face, and laughed again. “ Oh, I
understand you so well now, my dear friend, and
how I loathe myself that I ever believed in you !
I thought you were a man, you see, and you are
only a poor contemptible wisp of a thing. ”
“ Don’t say nasty things, Rose—you are play-
ing a losing game. You are playing pluckily, but
don’t be rash.”
She laughed again. “ How could I have thought
you a man ! I see you and all your paltry creep-
ing nature as clearly as ever woman could see
such things. How well I know how this rumor,
of which you speak, has originated, here a little,
there a little, to gratify your pride, and enhance
your reputation.”
‘ ‘ Reputation INBo you, for an instant, realise
how reckless you are when you talk of reputation ?
Do you remember that yours is at my mercy ? ”
“As is also that of every other woman of your
acquaintance. I realise this, that it is sad for any
of my sex to know you—sadder still if they happen
to attract you. But remember this : that I am
quite prepared to take the consequences of any act
of mine, and that, having been indiscreet, I will
have to suffer. The fault is my own. I do not
even blame you ; you are what you are, and some
instinct should have warned me of you. You
threaten me with scandal if I refuse the honor of
your further acquaintance : I certainly prefer the
scandal.”
“ It will be difficult not to believe scandal
against so charming a creature as you.”
She rose and went towards the door. “ I think
my wisest plan will be to tell my husband every-
thing, making a point of the fact that any gossip
or scandal that arises originated from you in
revenge for my refusing to let you make love to
me.”
He smiled. “You may be right; one or two
thing’s will be awkward to explain to a husband
possibly. ”
She flushed. “ Not to mine, for he is a man,
and were it a thousand times more difficult I
would prefer to do it; therefore, don’t you think
you’d better go before he comes in.”
“I daresay you are right: you so often are.
Adieu, sweet one! You’ve played your hand
well.”
